ACCORDING to a publication of the United
States Bureau of Statistics; the submarine tele
graphs of the world at the close of last Febru
ary numbered 1500, had an aggregate length of 170,
--000 miles, represented a cost estimated at $250,000,000,
and transmitted annually about 6,000.000 messages.
The greater number of these submarine telegraphs
are comparatively short cables owned by govern
ments and employed in connection with forts, light
houses and signal stations. There are about 320 Com
mercial cables, owned by thirty-five companies. These
control about 150,000 miles of cable, leaving for the
governmental cables only about 20,000 miles of .the
total. It is noted, however, that the governments of
the world hold something more than 80,060 miles of
cable stock for war purposes ready at hand to be laid
at a moment notice when the emergency arises.
Every great body of water lying between any inhab
ited parts of the earth has been crossed and recrosscd
by submarine telegraphs. The Mediterranean is trav
ersed through its length and breadth by numerous
lines, the Gulf of Mexico and the Caribbean Sea are
almost equally well supplied, and their islands arc con
nected with one another and with both the northern
and southern American continents; thirteen lines con
nect the United States with Europe; three span the
ocean between South America and the Old World;
the Red Sea and the Indian Ocean are traversed by
lines bringing Asia into communication with Europe;
while in the Pacific Ocean a scries of cables stretches
from Siberia southward to Australia and from that
continent other lines extend to New Zealand and to
New Caledonia.
The one link lacking to the completion of the world
system of submarine telegraph is that portion of the
Pacific extending from America across to Asia. Con
cerning that link the report says: "The chief obsta
cle in the past to the construction of a grand trans-
Pacific cable was found in the fact that midocean
resting places could not be satisfactorily obtained or
arranged for, no single government controlling a suf
ficient number of suitable landing places to make this
seem practicable in view of the belief that the distance
through which messages could be sent and cables con
trolled was limited."
The results of the war with Spain have put the
United States in a position to overcome the difficulty
cf the past. With landing places at Hawaii, Wake Is
land, Guam and the Philippines; we could lay a able
no section of which would have a length equal to that
now in daily operation between France and the United
States. The lengtht of the French cable from Brest to
Cape.Cod is 3250 miles, while the greatest distance
from land to land on the proposed Pacific route would
be the 2089 miles between San Francisco and Hawaii.
Along with the extension of ocean cables there has
been an extraordinary development and improvement
in the methods of their operation. On the first cable
across the Atlantic the speed rate was but three words
a minute, several hours were required for the trans
mission of a message and the receipt of a response,
and the cost to the sender was $100 a message. At
the present time the speed of transmission averages
fifty words, a minute, with a higher speed on the auto
matic transmitters; the price has been diminished to
25 cents per word, and it is noted that a message sent
from the House of Representatives in Washington to
the House of Parliament in London during the chess
match of 1898 was transmitted and a reply received
in 13^2 seconds. ly *''.;.
Summing up the benefits to be expected by the
United States from the construction of a trans-Pacific
cable, the report says: "The commerce of the coun
tries of Asia and Oceanica amounts to more than $2,
--000,000,000 annually, their imports alone averaging
more than $100,000,000 a month. Of this enormous
market the United States at present obtains less than
6 per cent. With direct cable communication across
the Pacific, direct water communication through the
Nicaragua Canal, and an increase in the number and
capacity of American steamships, it seems not im
proper to suppose a material addition might be made
to the share obtained by the United States in the trade
of that part of the world." ,-:.-,

A SON'S GRATITUDE.
TO the Editor of The Call: Permit me, through the columns of your
journal, to make public acknowledgment of my sincere gratitude and
heartfelt thanks to the people of San Francisco for their very
many courtesies, magnanimous and fraternal treatment of my hon
ored father â in life and in death and on the eve of my departure with
his remains for his loved home in Tennessee I beg to assure one and all
that a blessing is in my heart and a benediction on my lips for my
father's host of friends and acquaintances in this great city of the Pa
cific. In an especial manner do I desire to return my sincere thanks to
the three commanderies of Knights Templar for assuming entire charge
of the obsequies in this city and conducting them in the beautiful ritual
of "their order, which my father so much loved. To the , commanding
officer at the Presidio and of the National Guard for the escort of honer;
to the Hon. Samuel M. Shortridge for the words of eloquence; to the noble
women of the city who contributed the magnificent floral offerings, and
to the daily press for their kind words. The sadness attendant upon the
weary voyage across the Pacific finds here an amelioration which words
of mine can never express, and which I shall carry throughout life
among the choicest of memories. Very gratefully and respectfully,
GEORGE J. SMITH,
Sergeant Major First Tennessee Infantry, U. S. V.

According to Brainerd N. Rowley, editor
of California Fruit Grower, the fruit mi
i terests of the State are safe. He says
I "The outlook for good crops of fruit were
never better than they are at present. The
; fruit is fifteen days farther advanced than
lit was at this lime last year. The trees
are strong and hardy and there is little
possibility of their suffering a setback.
Cherries and apricots will not yield a full
crop, but all others will yield in abun
dance. It Is hard to tell what the foreign
I demand will be, as it is governed almost ,
I entirely by the price nt which the fruit
;is marketed. In this country, if the peo
ple want anything, they get it, giving no
' heed to the price. In other countries. If
they find an article costs more than they
I had set aside for it they go without or
; substitute something that can be had at
the price they intended to pay originally."
The infection of better times coming is
I in the air. and prosperity, which comes
i in the train of a largely increased volume
of trade, should soon be felt by the peo
! ple in all walks of life, whether they be
I in the ranks of the laborer or the mer-
I chant. ._

LAST HONORS
TO COL. SMITH
OF TENNESSEE
Just Tribute to the
Old Hero.
IMPRESSIVE MASONIC RITES
KNIGHTS TEMPLAR CONDUCT
THE FUNERAL SERVICES.
The Remains Escorted to the Ferry
by a Large Cortege of Police,
Military and Members of
â¢ the Order.
Grandly impressive were the honors
shown to the late Colonel William F.
Smith of Tennessee yesterdayâ nonors
such as are rarely shown the memory of
a fellow-soldier outside of his own home
circles. But though the widow and rela
tives of the veteran of two wars are
waiting for the home-coming of their
loved one, the breadth of the continent
away, it did not devolve upon mere stran
gers to perfunctorily perform the rites
due his rank in military and Masonic cir
cles. During his brief sojourn here last
summer, while en route to the Philip
pines, Colonel Smith made a host of
friendsâ new friends, to be sureâ but as
stanch and lasting as those he left be
hind in distant Tennessee.
It was with the expressed wish of these
new friends that the remains were held
here in order that San Francisco and the
State might honor them fittingly. ;'â 'â 
In his own State Colonel Smith was one
of the most prominent members* of the
Masonic order, and while here he was
welcomed into the local lodges, where he
formed many of .his warmest friendships.
It was proper, therefore, that the ser
vices should be conducted under the aus
pices of the Knights Templar, of with
deceased was past grand commander of
his native State.
The services were held in Masonic Tem
ple at i o'clock and it was significant of
the bonds that unite the North and South
that, among the large number of civilians
present .there were no more sincere
mourners than members of the Grand
Army and ladies of the Relief Corps, who
had learned to love this former officer in
the Confederate service.
Prominent in the temple were six com
rades of Colonel, then Captain Smith, of
the Army of Northern Virginia. They
were General H. R. D. Maclver of Stone
wall Jackson's staff and later a member
of the staff of J. E. B. Steuart, James E.
Tucker, Henry St. George Bryan, J. C.
Kane, J. S. Sherman and Thomas H. An
drews. In the escort to the ferry these
gentlemen were given a place in line im
mediately behind the members of the
Grand Commandery.
The heavy metallic casket, draped with
the American 'flag, stood in the center of
the floor and around and on top of it were
numerous beautiful floral pieces. Sir
Knight John F. Merrill presided, while
Rev. Alexander Carson officiated as pre
late. Sir Knight S. M. Shortridge deliv
ered the oration and Sir Knight Samuel
D. Mayer, grand organist and director of
the temple choir, arranged the musical
programme; which consisted of "One
Sweetly Solemn Thought," "Come to
Me," "Sleep, Brother, Sleep," and "Thy
Will Be Done," by the choir.
Besides the Grand Commandery there
were present California Commandery No.
1, Golden Gate No. 16 and Oakland No. 11,
all in full regalia, and these, together
with a large detachment of troops, acted
as escort.
The troops, drawn up on Post street in
front of the Temple, attracted an im
mense concourse of people, and for an
hour or more the street was almost im
passable. Sir Knight Frank V. Sumner
had charge of the escort, the troops be
ing commanded by Captain James E.
Britt, Twenty-fourth United States In
fantry. '
At 4 o'clock the services were concluded
and the casket was borne to the hearse,
the troops and Knights presenting arms
and the mounted band of the Fourth
United States Cavalry playing "Nearer
My God, to Thee." *
The pallbearers were Sir Knights
Franklin H. Day and Brilsford P. Flint
for California Commandery; Z. F. Gilpin
and A. W. Burrell for Oakland Com
mandery; Charles L. Field and A G
Booth for Golden Gate Commandery and
Majors H. B. Hosmer and James A
Margo of General Warfield's staff repre
senting the National Guard of California
Lieutenant Birdsall and a platoon of
thirty police headed the procession and
were followed by the band and Troops B
and M, Fourth United States Cavalry
.Troop A and the Second Brigade Signal
Corps, N. G. C. Behind the mounted j
troops marched Companies E and I
Twenty-fourth United States Infantry!
and it was a source of comment on the
changed conditions of the country that
colored troops marched in honor of one
who a third of a century ago fought to
continue their race in bondage. But these
dusky heroes of El Caney, Siboney and
Santiago were proud to pay this tribute
to the gallant old soldier who had now
fallen for the same flag they fought
under. Following the troops came the
three commanderies of Knights Templar,
the hearse followed by the dead officer's
charger he brought with him from Ten
nessee and left behind when he went to
Manila. The somber trappings, boots re
versed in the stirrups and sheathed sword
hanging from the pommel of the saddle
but added to the solemnity of the occa
sion. :-."â â '- --:-â .. r'i.> :.--^l:~:. '/--.,' :
The Â«Grand Commandery, Confederate
Veterans, and carriages containing Ser
geant George Smith, son of the dead offi
cer, who is accompanying the remains
home, and several lady friends of the
family brought up the rear.
Down Market street to Steuart, to Mis
sion, and thence to the eastern gate to
the depot marched the cortege, the mili
tary forming line and presenting arms at
the gate, while the Knights continued on
to the dock. A delegation went across
the bay to Oakland, where the casket was
placed on board the Central overland and
started east at 6 o'clock. Sergeant
Smith's mother and Bister will meet him
at St. Louis and with him will accompany
the remains of their husband and father
to Nashville.
A Fighting Artist.
E. M. Rinaldo, an artist, was yesterday
held to answer before the Superior Court
by Judge Mogan on the charge' of assault
with a deadly weapon In $1000 bonds. Dur
ing a row on Sacramento street, near
Kearny, he struck a man on the temple
with a bottle, cutting -an artery.

April 11.- 1899.
To the Honorable the Board of .election
Commissionersâ Gentlemen: I respectfully ask
your honorable board to authorize me. to pre
pare for the holding of the primary election in
accordance with the act approved March S.
1.99, and known as the "Stratton Primary
Law." All elections under this law are to be
conducted, managed and controlled as are
elections for State and county officers.: The
general precincts as they now exist may be
consolidated, but not more than three- shall
constitute a primary election precinct. Regis
tration for this election will be open not-later
than June 3 and close July 22. The election will
be held on Tuesday, August 8, 1899. v.;*-
It will be necessary to map out the primary
election precincts, to prepare the necessary
blanks, tally lists, credentials and returns for
approval by your honorable board.
It will also be necessary to prepare for the
municipal election, which will take place in
November next, in accordance with the charter.
All election laws have been changed, with ref
erence to registration, and a new form of bal
lot and ballot law has been adopted.
Registration is now open, In view of the fact
that there may be an election for the ' issu
ance of bonds for local improvements.
I would therefore, in view of the foregoing,
ask your honorable board to be allowed the
necessary assistance as will from time to time
be required from now on for the proper conduct
of this office. Respectfully, ' â¢._ :â 'â 
W. J. BIGGY, Registrar of Voters.
All of the requests made by the Regis
trar were granted. Mr. Biggy stated that
he would be able to conduct registration .
for the bond, primary and municipal elec- -\
tions with his present clerical force and
without additional expense to the city.
New registration will not be necessary to
enable voters to participate in these elec
tions except where changes of residence
have occurred. . '('. ->:â â¢â¢-

MAJORITYâ J. D. H., City. If two
candidates run for an office, 120 votes are
cast, one receives 59 votes and the other
61 votes, the one receiving the highest
number is elected by a majority of two
votes. In that case it would require 61
votes to elect. Receiving that number,
one has a majority of two votes over his
opponent, who received but 59.

ticular department he would like to
work in. ' V.r-"

GARRISON DUTY
AT HONOLULU
Four Batteries Sixth
Artillery Depart.
A CLUBBING CAUSES INSANITY
BILL BOONAKA, A SAILOR ON
THE MAUNA ALA, BEATEN.
Sailing of the Nippon Maru for the
Orientâ A "Wharf Rat" Drowned
From Lombard-Street
Wharf.
The Toyo Risen Kaisha's steamer Nip
pon Maru sailed for the Orient yesterday
crowded with passengers and loaded to
tha guards with frieght. For Honolulu
she took away four batter! - of tl/e Sixth
Artillery. They will relieve the Second
Battery. U. S. V.. which has been sta
tioned In Hawaii almost since the an
nexation of the islands. The latter on
their return here will be mustered out of
the service. The batteries which sailed
on tne Nippon Maru are in command of
Major Samuel Miles.
Miss Ida M. Forsyth was also a pas
senger by the Nippon. Maru. On her ar
rival at Honolulu she will report to the
surgeon in charge ot the United States
Military Hospital for duty as active chief
nurse.
The Jeanette Waldorf Company also
went away on the steamer. They will till
an engagement at Honolulu and will then
proceed to Hongkong on the next China
steamer.
"Bill" Boonaka. a sailor on the Hawai
ian bark Mauna Ala, has gone crazy as
the result of a beating he received in the
"Scandinavian headquarters" on the cor
ner of Jackson and Montgomery avenue
last Tuesday morning. He was struck
over the head with a club and thrown
out on the sidewalk to shift for himself.
He was picked up in a semi-cons
condition and sent to the Harbor Receiv
ing Hospital in the patrol wagon. On his
arrival there Hr. yon der Leith sewed up
a lacerated wound over the eye and also
two wounds on the scalp. The sailor was
drunk, as well as being badly hurt, so he
was put to bed.
About noon Boonaka was considerably
better, and when he said be wanted to
leave the hospital no attempt was made
to detain him. He was not seen again
until Officer Mackey ran across him on
the front yesterday. He was then a rav
ing maniac and it took half a dozen po
licemen to handle him. At the Receiving
Hospital in the City Hall It was found
the clubbing received in the Scandinavian
headquarters had affected his brain. He
was placed in a strait jacket and sent
out to the City and County Hospital.
Boonaka is a Russian-Finn and lived at
Lane's sailor boarding house on Davis
street. Bane says he always was a quiet,
peaceable fellow, but he must have got in
with a tough crowd at the saloon.
One of the "wharf rats" is missing and
the harbor police are rounding up the
tribe to see which of them lost his life.
Early yesterday morning several of them
appeared on Bombard street wharf and
made a raid on some potatoes that were
stored there. As potatoes are retailing at
o cents a pound, a sack meant a good
haul to a wharf rut. One of them was
surprised in the act of lowering a sack
into a small skiff by Fred Raabe. When
the latter began to run down the wharf
the young fellow pot confused, and los
ing his balance fell into the bay. He
must have struck against the skiff, as
both he and the potatoes disappeared.
The wharf rat did not come up again and
a few bubbles were all that showed Raabe
where he had gone down. The rickety
skiff which he used in his midnight
prowls is still tied up to Lombard street
The Marine Firemen's Union is mak
ing another attempt to get rid of the
Chinese employed on the United States
transport Hancock (late Arizona). With
this enrl in view the union has interested
the (. alifornia delegation to Congress and
a hard fight Is to be made to get white
firemen work on United States vessels.
The association made the attempt some
Â« me ago. but failed, because at that time
Lncle Sam did not own the vessel. The
facts of the case have been wired to the
Secretary of War and it is hoped that
the Chinese will be replaced by white
men before the Hancock sails.

A BUSY DAY AT PACIFIC-STREET WHARF.
THE work of getting the United States transport Hancock (late Arizona) ready for another voyage
to Manila is going ahead with a rush. The vessel came into port with the name Arizona on her bows
and on her stern, but these have been removed and the name Hancock substituted. She came in a
black-painted steamer, but will sail again as one of the white squadron. The British ship Strathdon is ly
ing alongside the Hancock coaling the latter vessel. â  On the south side of the wharf is the Australian mail
steamer Mariposa and astern of her is the island trader Mauna Ala. Into all these vessels freight is be
ing rushed, so Pacific-street wharf is as busy as a bee-hive from morning till night.

he therefore vacated the order made last
Tuesday and set May 4th as the date for
! hearing the application for the extension
! of time in which to complete the taking of
testimony before the Master in Chancery.
A SPECIAL EXCURSION TO THE
NOTED CAMPING RESORTS OF
THE SANTA CRUZ MOUNTAINS,
TO BE GIVEN UNDER THE AUS
PICES OF THE SOUTHERN
PACIFIC COMPANY.
Splendid Trains of First-Class Equip
ment and a Very Low
Rate.
Many years ago the Southern Pacific
Company inaugurated a series of per
sonally conducted excursions to the
noted resort places of the Santa Cruz
Mountains and the famous Shasta re
gion, the same being given under the
auspices of the passenger department
of the company, who appointed Colonel
William H. Menton to the position of
excursion passenger agent in charge
of same.
We are advised by the passenger de
partment that for the current season
they will follow out the custom here
tofore in vogue and it is now an
nounced that the company will run one
or more first-class trains to the Santa
Cruz Mountains on Sunday, April 30.
As heretofore these trains will be un
der the personal supervision of Colonel
Menton, the well-known excursion pas
senger agent, who will see to it that
excursionists are well cared for.
These excursion trains will stop at
the following well-known camping re
sorts situated on the line of the
Coast Division Narrow-Gauge: Alma,
Wrights, Laurel, Glenwood, Campus,
Felton, Ben Lomond, Rowardennan and
Boulder Creek. Excursionists desiring
to spend their time at any one of the
above points can do so and take ex
cursion train, which will stop for them
on return trip. Round-trip rate $1 25.
Train will leave San Francisco ferry
landing, 7:15 a. m. ; Oakland (Four
teenth and Franklin streets), 7:45 a. m.;
Alameda (Park street), 8:20 a. m. Re
turning, arrive at San Francisco at 8:05
I p. m.
Tickets will be on sale at 613 Market
street, Grand Hotel Ticket Office, April
27, 28 and 29, and at ferry landing on
1 the morning of the excursion. They
! can also be procured at Fourteenth and
Franklin streets, Oakland, and at Park
street station, Alameda, on the dates
j above named.
. THE SAN FRANCISCO 7 CALL, THURSDAY, APRIL 13, 1899.
NEALL MIGHT
HAVE TURNED
IN THE CHECKS
Damaging Evidence
at His Trial.
INDORSEMENT NOT NEEDED
SLAVENS WOULD HAVE TAKEN
THEM AS THEY WERE.
First Bad Day for the OfficerâTesti
mony Relating to the Most
Serious Charge Against
Him.
Yesterday was not so good a day for
Lieutenant Neall as has been the others
of his trial. The first really damaging
testimony against him was given, and the
questions of the defense could not make
much out of it except the fact that it
might have been worse. And the most
serious part of it was that the testimony
was given upon and tends to prove the
gravest of the chargesâ conduct unbecom
ing an officer and a gentleman.
Lieutenant Harris, adjutant of the
Fourth Cavalry and of the post during
the time of Neall's trouble, continued on
the stand until near the hour of adjourn
ment. His examination related mostly to
the statement made to him by Neall that
it was necessary to obtain the indorse
ment of Mrs. Nolan to certain checks be
fore he could turn over the accounts of
the post exchange. In making this state
ment it was alleged that Neall willfully
deceived the adjutant, and the witness
would not recede from this view of the
matter.
The witness said he had no knowledge
of whether or not Neall knew of the ap
pointment of Mrs. Nolan as administra
trix of her husband's estate when he said
he had to await her action on the checks
made payable to her husband as officer
of the exchange. Neither had he any
knowledge whether or not Neall, knowing
Mrs. Nolan had been appointed, willfully
neglected to secure her indorsement. He
believed he had been deceived first be
cause Neall refused to turn over to his
successor the checks needing indorse
ment; second, because while the amount
under his control in money and property
made up a total of over $4900, the checks
he referred to did not amount to more
than $200 at the most; third, because ho
had been informed by the post com
mander that Neall had made the same
excuse to him, for his failure to turn over
the books. And during all this time his
successor had signified a willingness to
accept the checks as they were and se
cure the indorsement himself.
"Do you believe from the facts as you
see them that Lieutenant Neall intended
to deceive you?" asked Mr. Thornton.
"I do," was Harris' reply.
"Do you admit the explanation was
true in fact?"
"No, I do not."
"Do you admit it was partly true?"
"No, I do not."
"Do you admit it was true In any
sense?"
"1 do not believe It was a true excuse
for his neglect."
Harris then went on to say there was
no reason why Neall should not have paid
the difference between these checks and
the amount that should have been on
hand toward the dividends to the regi
mental fund and the band fund. Had he
known the. checks amounted to only $200
or less he would have made an investi
gation at once. As It was, he took what
Nf all told him as the truth. "I know now
it was not the truth," were the closing
words of the witness.
Thomas H. Slavens, first lieutenant,
Fourth Cavalry, quartermaster of the
post and of the regiment, commissary of
the post and of the regiment, officer of
the post exchange, police officer of the
post and fire marshal of the post, was
the next witness. He had succeeded to
the job vacated by Neall. He testified
that when the accounts of the commis
sary office were turned over to him they
were correct, but there was money owing
to the department. One of these bills
was from Neall himself. There was also
due the treasurer of the post his allot
ment of the receipts from the commis
sary store. There was also due the bill
of the post exchange to the commissary
department.
The witness .then told how he had taken
possession of the post exchange on Feb
ruary 16â the day after Neall's disappear
anceâand how he had found things in a
rather chaotic condition. There were out
standing bills amounting to over $3000 and
the account at the bank had been over
drawn about $150. He received from the
adjutant a bundle Of checksâ checks
which Neall told him had to be indorsed
by Mrs. Nolanâ and these he took to her
and had them Indorsed and afterward
deposited In the bank. They did not
amount to more than $200. It was some
time In February when Neall told him
he could not get the checks Indorsed. It
was in January Mrs. Nolan was appoint
ed administratrix of her husband's estate
and became capable of indorsing the
checks.
"The checks could have been turned
over to me without Indorsement," said
the witness, in answer to questions, "and
I could have secured the indorsement my
self, as I ultimately did."
The hour of adjournment cut off for the
time being questions on the financial af
fairs of the club.
The evidence relating to the indorse
ment of the checks is damaging, for upon
it is based three of the specifications in
the charge of conduct unbecoming an offi
cer and a gentleman.
LOVE TRIUMPHS OVER
MATERNAL OPPOSITION
MANUEL RIVAS AND INACIA
TURRES ARE MAN AND WIFE.
They Eloped, Pursued by Mrs. Turres,
but They Were Just Ten Min
utes Ahead of Her.
An Irate mother, accompanied by a male
friend, called at the Chief of Police's of
fice yesterday morning and wanted her
daughter and her lover arrested. She had
reason to believe they had eloped and
might possibly seek to get married, but
she desired the marriage prevented.
The mother was Mrs. Turns, 2T0% Bran
nan street, and she said her daughter was
too young to marry, as she was only 14
years of age last October. The young man
was Manuel Kivas, who was employed in
an office as shipping clerk.
Detective Anthony was detailed on the
case and he went to "Cupid" Danforth's
office, where he ascertained that a mar
riage license had been issued to Manuel
Rlvas, 20 years of age, and Inacia Turres,
18 years of age. He was told that Kivas
and the girl had sworn that she was of
age, and as she is a well developed young
girl there was no suspicion that she was
younger.
The detective learned that the couple
seemed to be in a hurry to get married,
and he called upon different Justices of
the Peace, and Justice Cook informed
him that he had performed the ceremony
about ten minutes previous and the young
couple had hurried away.
Mrs. Torres was waiting in the Chief's
offlce and was furious when Anthony ap
prised' her of the result of his investiga
tion. She announced her determination to
get even with Rlvas and will annul the
marriage if possible. To-day, unless wiser
counsels prevail,, she will swear out a
warrant for the arrest of Rlvas on the
charge of perjury for swearing that her
daughter was IS years of age when she is
only a few months past 14.

Pulled Her Back Hair.
Lizzie Booth, a domestic at 3621 Eight
eenth street, swore to a complaint yes
terday in Judge Graham's court for the
arrest of Louis Mooser, 2103 Vallejo street,
on a charge of battery, Lizzie was em

that Saturday he struck her and seized
her by the back hair and dragged her
along for several feet. She left his em
ployment that day.

A CHINESE MAN AND AN
I NDIAN MAIDEN TO MARRY.
A CHINESE man and an Indian maiden are the latest novelty in the
field of matrimony. They visited Cupid Danforth at the City Hall yes
terday and procured a license permitting them to wed. Miss Neevis
Paderas is the happy bride-to-be, and she is as comely a young wo
man as one could meet in a day's journey. She Is just 18 years old and
very much in love with Lee Moy, her intended husband. Lee Moy is a
young Chinese, sleek, prosperous and extremely" proud of his ability to speak
English like a native. Long ago he decided to make. America his perma
nent home, so he cut off his queue, doffed the costume of his own country,
forswore lottery, fan tan and the opium pipe and became as good an Ameri
can citizen as it Is possible for him to be. Feeling that it was not good for
man to be alone, he looked about him for a.'wife, and found his affinity in
comely Neevis Paderas. To her he paid court, escorted her to and from
church in the Mission district, confessed his love and was accepted.
Both Mr. Moy and Miss Paderas have a good common school education
and both are members of the Methodist church. They applied to Clerk Dan
forth last week for a license, but that official was not sure that he was per
mitted by law to issue a license in such a case, so they were told to come
again yesterday. In the meantime it was found that the law which pro
hibits the intermarriage of individuals of the Caucasian race with Chinese,
negroes or Indians was silent as to marriages between Chinese and Indians.
So it was that when the young couple arrived at the City Hall they were
given a license. They declared their intention of being married by a Meth
odist minister.
DRUGGIST GATES WAS
ASPHYXIATED IN BED
FOUND DEAD IN A ROOM AT THE
LICK.
Was Engaged to Be Married to Miss
Ida Kleinhaus and Had Been in
Her Company the Even
ing Before.
A. L. Gates, druggist at the Folsom
Penitentiary, was asphyxiated in room
153 of the Lick House some time last Tues
day night, and his dead body was found
by a bellboy at 7 o'clock yesterday morn
ing. Deputy Coroner McCormick removed
the body to an undertaking establishment
and made an inquiry into the cause of
death. He reported that there was no
doubt that the death was accidental. Be
yond the fact that the bellboy had found
Gates dead with the stopcock of the gas
jet turned on Mr. McCormick seemed to
know nothing. The hotel clerk had an
equally ample fund of information. He
said that Gates registered at the hotel last
Tuesday night ana that he had been found
dead in the morning. The Keyhole and
other crevices in the room had not been
stopped up, and this fact was considered
as evidence that the case was not one of
suicide. _ , __ .
An hour after the discovery of the body
Miss Ida Kleinhaus of 640 Fell street
called at the hotel and inquired for Gates.
When Informed that he was dead she
burst into tears and was inconsolable. She
said that Gates had made an appointment
to meet her at S o'clock. She went away
and returned shortly afterward to the ho
tel with her brother. He stated to Dep
uty Coroner McCormick that his sister
and Mr. Gates had been keeping company
for several months and were to have been
married in a few weeks. He saw Gates
on the previous evening on his return
home from the theater sitting in the par
lor of the Kleinhaus residence with Miss
Kleinhaus. He retired after greeting the
visitor and was informed by his sister
yesterday that Gates left about midnight.
Gates left word with the night clerk at
the hotel to be called at 7 o'clock in the
morning, adding that he expected a
friend to meet him at 8 o'clock.
The denial of the hotel clerk that the
key of the gas fixtures was loose deepens
the mystery. The most rational supposi
tion is that the deceased when he turned
off the gas carelessly turned the key
partly on again.
Young Gates was 28 years old and had
been for several years employed in the
prison as -druggist. He came down from
Folsom last Tuesday.

A NOTORIOUS CHARACTER.
Chris Myrtle, an Ex-Convict, Arrest
ed on a Charge of Burglary.
Chris Myrtle, ex-convict and member of
a notorious family, was arrested early
yesterday morning and booked at the City
Prison on a charge of burglary. He was
caught in the room of F. A. Dunbar, 1346
Market street, just as he was about to
leave with a bundle of clothes he had
gathered together.
Myrtle while serving a term for burg
lary in San Quentin was the leader of a
gang -that made a break for liberty sev
eral years ago. His father and mother
were arrested some years ago for mur
der in Spanishtown. After the jury
brought in a verdict of guilty against the
woman her husband confessed that he
was the murderer, but he was not be
lieved, and she was sent to San Quentin.
Dan. one of the sons, was shot and killed
at Twelfth and Harrison streets, and Joe,
another son, has only recently served six
months for failure to provide.
Death of William Friel.
William Freel, a stevedore, whose resi
dence was at 32 Tehama street, died yes
terday morning from injuries received a
few days ago by the fall of a coal buck
et upon him In the hold of a ship In
which he was working. The case has
been reported to the Coroner.
Unknown Drowned Man.
The body of an unknown man was found
yesterday floating in the bay near the foot
of Washington street wharf. The body
had not been more than a day in the wa
ter. The deceased was about 50 years old.
5 feet S Inches tall, weight about 160
pounds, blue eyes, light hair turning: gray,
red chin whiskers and mustache. He
wore a black cutaway coat, gray pants
and no vest, negligee shirt of black with
white stripes, and laced shoes. The body
remains at the Morgue for identification.
A Chinese Capper.
Chung Gom was ordered into custody
by Judge Graham yesterday on a charge
of vagrancy. He had been observed
hanging around the court for several
days and talking to Chinese prisoners in
the dock. Yesterday Ah Sun was in the
dock on a charge of selling lottery tick
ets and when the Judge observed Chung
Gom talking to him he ordered Sergeant
Duncan to arrest him. Ah Sun was fined
$50 or fifty days.

78
MANUFACTURERS AND
PRODUCERS FEAST
Almost Two Hundred Sat Down to the Annual
Banquet at the Merchants' Club.
The Welfare of San Francisco and California Was the
Keynote of the Sentiment Expressed in
Response to the Toasts,
THE members of the Manufacturers'
and Producers' Association and their
guests, men prominent in the com
mercial and professional life of the
city, sat down last night l-> the annual
banquet in the large dining room of the
Merchants' Club, on the eighth lloor of
the Mutual Life building. Nearly 200
gentlemen extended their limbs under the
table to discuss a menu that fully war
ranted the close attention they bestowed
and fully prepared them for the oratorical
feast that came after the cigars were
lighted.
At the head of the long center table sat
A. Sbarboro, the newly elected president
of the association, who acted as toast
master. On his right sat Mayor Phelan
und on his left C. P. Huntington, each of
whom made responses during the evening
that elicited the greatest merriment. The
keynote to the sentiment of the evening
was "The Welfare of San Francisco," and
to pave the way for those who were to
follow him and point out the means to
be used to attain that end James W.
Kerr, the retiring president, was culled
upon to respond to the toast, "The Work
of the Manufacturers' and Producers' As
sociation. He said, in part:
"In the light of recent events the future
of the Pacific Coast and more particularly
San Francisco has been the subject of
much thought and much discussion. We
have had ideas advanced and exchanged
on this all-absorbing topic. This city 'Â«
bound to grow to be one of the largest
centers in the world. 1 am not mv of
an advocate of the idea of sitting down
and seeing things grow. 1 believe oppor
tunities are present here and that great
developments will take place in the near
future, which will enable us by energetic
work to make San Francisco all you ex
pert and would like it to be. But you
must build, not wait.
"The merchant and manufacturer must
join hands to build up the industries of
the State of California. They must see
to it that this city of San Francisco be no
longer a way station between the manu
facturers of the East and the markets of
the Orient. They must .see. to it that this
city must become a base of supplies. To
place San Franlcsco in !hi. independent
position Is the object of the Manufactur
ers' and Producers' Association, and that
Is the aim and the end of the home indus
try movement in which you are all re
spectfully invited to co-operate."
The health of President McKinley, re
ferred i" by th- toastmaster as the great
est man on earth, was drunk standing.
Congressman-elect Julius Kahn being
called upon to respond. He did so in a
speech that was loudly and frequently
applauded.
"When we speak of our President." he
said, "we can say that when he assumed
the helm of state he found our manufac
turers and producers looking for markets
in vain; he found, indeed, 'sloth in the
mart'; he found the factories closed and
Coxey armies tramping throughout the
land. in two years what do we see? He
has recreated conditions and to-day our
workingmc-n are getting increased wages
and we see in the newspapers reports of
this factory and that factory raising the
hire of Its employes and the balance of
trade in favor of our country during this
last year of six hundred and eighty mil
lions of dollars. Why, gentlemen, if that
condition were continued for another ten
years this Government of ours, this people
of ours, this nation that we all love, will
be the creditor nation of the world. I am
satisfied that even his political opponents
will admit that William McKinley was
the right man at the right time."
Attorney General Tirey L. Ford, who
said he was not a producer in the or
dinary acceptation of the word, respond
ed to the toast, "The State of California."
"Never before," he said, "has the State
been so signally favored as now since
the days of '49. The eyes of the world
are turned to the Golden West and, as
had been suggested by Mr. Kerr, the
State is upon the threshold of a new era.
Logical events had placed California in
the very' forefront, and with all eyes
turned to the Orient California's hand is

on the nation's gateway ready to wel
come the commerce of the Far East."
In introducing Mayor Phelan to respond
to the toast. "San Francisco," Mr. Sbar- j
boro painted a dream of the city's future
greatness, which included free salt water
baths in every household. The Mayor,
after avowing that his thunder had been
appropriated, continued:
"The chairman has announced the mv- |
nicipal programme. He forgot but one
thing. In addition to the salt water baths
we are going to have an ordinance pro
viding for compulsory bathing. He re
minds me of another time when a dis
tinguished gentleman. Sir Thomas More, j
made such a municipal programme and I
published his researches under the title I
of 'Utopia.' You will recall his fate. He j
was beheaded."
The remainder of the Mayor's remarks j
were devoted to the grand opportunity i
within the grasp of this city and State !
given by the acquisition of the Philippines j
and he took occasion to say that as a
San Franciscan he felt that the views of j
Thomas Jefferson concerning the acquisi
tion of outlying territory were not fitted i
for these times.
Senator Perkins responded to the toast.
"Our Country." and during the course of â 
his remarks he took occasion to say that \
he, too. could see the great future before j
this city and State and the manufactur
ers and producers. He gloried In the j
achievements of the Oregon and other j
warships turned out on this coast, and i
then he paid a tribute to C. P. Hunting
ton. In it he said that the Southern Pa- I
cific magnate had done more for the State I
than any other six men in it.
The toastmaster called upon Mr. Hunt- |
ington to say something about railroads, I
and the aged railroader acquiesced.'
"I am not one of the scholarly men of
the country. I am not In literature, or
high art. I have been sawing wood for
seventy years. I feel myself honored In
being here to-night. I don't know as I
could say anything except that Mr. Per
kins, my friend, has said something in
favor of myself, for the very first time, I
believe. As he says, you people In San
Francisco have done great things. You !
ran an opposition steamer line to Panama
at the cost of a few hundred thousand
dollars and made millions. You should do
it some more. There are many things for
Californians to. do, and the little things
are the ones to do. I am always doing
something.
"I am not afraid that the star-spangled
banner will be torn down by any nation. â 
The great American system will stand. We
have gone to Manila. I'm glad we have.
And we have a position In the world we
must maintain. Some said we should nit
have gone to the tropics, but we did go
there, and if we can go on raising those
people up we will be doing a good thing."
William Sproule. freight traffic manager
of the Southern Pacific Company, spoke
of the "Railroad's Relations to Com
merce," and achieved the distinction of j
having made the speech of the evening. I
The relations of the railroads with com- i
merce, he said, were satisfactory, not- I
withstanding the fact that one might be
induced by reading the papers that run
down everything to think otherwise. The
railroads, he continued, seemed to him to
be the poor relations of commerce. He \
told some pertinent stories to illustrate \
his remarks and brought forth much j
laughter.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, APRIL 13,* 1899.
BADGE FOR SHERIFF MARTIN
Popular Official Honored by His Associates
at a Banquet.
SHERIFF HENRY S. MARTIN was presented with a handsome diamond
badge last evening by the deputies in his office. The presentation took
place at an elaborate banquet given at Pabst Cafe, at which fifty
guests assembled. The badge is a beautiful speciment of the jeweler's
art and was made by Alfred Liebert, a personal friend of Mr. Martin. On
the face is a laurel wreath in green gold, which surrounds a Bis-pointed
star, with a three-karat diamond in the center. The whole is surmounted by
an eagle In etruscan gold, with a smaller diamond for the eye. On a plain
circle of gold "Sheriff, San Francisco." is enameled in blue. On the reverse
side of the badge Is the following Inscription: "Presented to Henry S. Mar
tin as a token of esteem and respect by his associates."
Sheriff's Attorney Edgar D. Peixotto made the presentation speech in a
complimentary vein, and Mr. Martin responded briefly, though feelingly. As
he aptly put it, "The Sheriff is a man of few words, but of much execu
tion."
Toastmaster Peixotto called on ex-Sheriff Richard Whelan, who spoke
on "How the Sheriff's Office Should Be Run," and recounted some amusing
incidents which occurred when he transferred the jails Over to his successor
last January.
Under Sheriff David C. Martin, to whom the credit for the successful
outcome of the affair was largely due, told of the "Duty of the Subordi
nate." Toasts were also responded to by Recorder Albert C. Berthier, Jules
S. Godeau and others. ' "__V;",
Besides the employes in the office the honored guests present were Louis
Carraine, T. Gay, A. Goustiaux. E. C. Mailhebouou and J. S. Godeau. .

A Crazy Picture Dealer.
Otto Frensdorff, a picture dealer, went
into the photographic gallery of. W.
Johnston, 55 Third street, yesterday and
stole a purse belonging to Mrs. Lena
Franklin, the cashier, while she was ab
sent from the office for a moment, lie
returned later and stele two dozen as
sorted photographs and was arrested by
Detectives Ryan and O'Dea. Frensdorff
about a week ago swore to a warrant for
the arrest of three women whom he
charged ha.l robbed him, but it was
learned that he was laboring under a
hallucination and he was sent before the
Insanity Commissioners, but was dis
charged. ; V'<-

THEIR GRAND SESSION

CURRENT TERM.
A Peculiar Case That Calls for a Judi
cial Construction of the Law
of the Order.
At the second session of the Grand
Council of the Order of Chosen Friends
yesterday morning the consideration of
I the proposition of reduction of time
when the assessments be forwarded to
the* proper officers was resumed, and
after a lengthy discussion was adopted.
The grand councilor appointed Past
Grand Councilors J. M. Fulweiler, A. B.
Sanborn, Mrs. Henry Jr_.yOt, T. H. Sel
vc-ge". Daniel So well, C. M. Arnold and
H. W. Hutton a committee to draw up
resolution:- to the memory of L. Caro of
San Bernardino Council, grand trustee,
and Its report was adopted.
The report of the committee on laws
and supervisions was adopted.
The salary of the grand treasurer was
allowed to remain as at present. The
contingent fund for the grand councilor
was fixed at $1000.

preme representatives. Four ballots were

Sweeney is the first lady member of the
order who has been accredited to the Su
preme Council as a representative. _^
As alternates there were chosen: Dan
iel Sewell of Samaritan Council, E. R.
Teil of Friendship Council, A. O. Carpen
ter of Ukiah Council, A. B. Sanborn of
Jackson Council and Mrs. E. C/ Gorman
of Sacramento Council. . ;'â _â¢ â¢_â¢:
Then followed elections with the fol
lowing result: William H. Savage of
Martha Washington Council of San Pe
dro, grand councilor; D. J. Hartley of
Sacramento Council, grand assistant
councilor; Dr. R. S. Markell of Amico
Council, Cloverdale, grand vice coun
cilor; S. C. Wallis of Evans Council, San
Francisco, grand recorder (re-elected);
Miss Clara McDonald of Social Council,
San Francisco, grand treasurer; Mrs.
Lizzie Morse of Rainbow Council, Marys
ville, grand prelate; Mrs. Jennie Pratt of
Evans Council, San Francisco, grand
marshal; Mrs. M. A. Boarland of Stand
ard Council, Bodie. grand warden; Mrs.
Minnie Lakin of San Rafael Council,
grand guard; Mrs. A. Boehm of Golden
Gate Council, San Francisco, grand sen
try; E. A. Webber of Guardian Council,
San Jose, trustee for short term; J. i .
McDonnell of Comstock Council, \irgima
City, trustee for the long term.
It was resolved that it was the sense of
the Grand Council that medical examin
ers should be beneficiary members of the
order. . It : " 'â¢'.-' _^- â
The next session of the Grand Council
will be held in San Francisco in 1901.
It was resolved that a premium of 12 be
paid to a member who brings in a candi
date, but not until after he shall have
paid two assessments. â¢ ,
It was decided to appropriate $300 to help
defray the expenses of the approaching
twentieth anniversary of the order.
A vote of thanks was tendered to the
Progressive Councils for its reception on
Tuesday night, and one to the press and
particularly to The Call for its courtesy
to the order in reports of its proceedings.
A vote of thanks was also extended to
the retiring grand councilor for his ear
nest work in behalf of the order during
his term of office.
A very peculiar case occupied the at
tention of the Grand Council for some time.
A member of Rainbow Council of Marys
ville. Margaret Carr, made her son her
beneflciarv in the sum of $3000. The son
died suddenly, and three days later, be
fore Mrs. Carr had an opportunity to
change her beneficiary, she died. The widow
of the son claimed the money, and so did
two sisters of Mrs. Carr, and one of her
nieces also claimed the money. The law
of the order says that "should all the
beneficiaries selected by the member die
before the decease of such member the
benefit shall be paid to the heirs of the
deceased member dependent upon him or
her; and if no person or persons shall be
entitled to receive such benefit by the laws
of this order it shall revert to the relief
fund." Upon inquiry it was discovered
that none of the claimants were dependent
upon Mrs. Carr. and the claim was not
allowed to either. The matter was re
ferred to the supreme vice councilor and
grand recorder, to prepare a full state
ment of the case and law for transmission
to Rainbow Council. The matter will no
doubt be brought into court to have the
law of the order juaicially determined, as
the order is ready to pay the money as
soon as it can be ascertained who is en
titled to it.
The officers elected for tho current two
vear term were then installed by Past
Supreme Councilor C. M. Arnold, and the
session was closed after addresses by the
new officers.
To-night the grand officers and repre
sentatives will be the guests of Evans
and Golden Gate councils at a reception
to be given in the Social Hall of the For
esters' building.
OLYMPIC'S RACE MEET.
Eiders Have About Completed Their
Active Training.
The cycle racers are putting the finish
ing touches to their training, preparatory
to the bit* race meet to be held by the j
Olympic Club "Wheelmen next Sunday at
their outside grounds on H street, south
of the park. The blue line of electric
cars "pass the door." so that the track is
readily accessible from downtown. There
is a big entry list, and the handicaps for
the open events will be announced to
morrow. Official Handicapper R. A.
Smyth is now worrying over them.
The club in promoting this meet is do
ing a great deal for the sport here and
should have the hearty support of all in
terested in the game. Some innovations
are promised, which will add interest to
the game, and the fact that the Olympic
Wheelmen are handling it is a guarantee
of a large attendance and a good show.
The officials for the meet will be: Judges
âJames E. Little, Capital City Wheel
men; Frank H. Kerrigan, Bay City
Wheelmen; E. 13. Jerome. Reliance Club;
A. P. Swain, Acme; Charles Albert Ad
ams. Olympic. Timersâ George P. Wet
mere, Bay City: Clinton R. Coulter,
Olympic: Tony Delmas, Garden City:
John McGlynn, Olympic. Scorersâ A. J.
Menne, Bay City; Joseph S. Lewis, Road
Club; Frank Bush, California Club. Clerk
â W. L. Loos, Olympic; assistant. J. F.
Hancock, Bay City. Marshalsâ George
Meihlir.g and W. B. Wegener. Announcer
âGeorge E. Dixon, Bay City. Starter-
John Elliott Olympic. Refereeâ Frank W.
Fuller, Olympic.
Who Owns Yellow TailP
The preliminary examination of Fred
| Foster, the horseman, charged with ob
taining money by false pretenses from A.
Howard, the Australian plunger, was
continued before Judge Conlan yesterday.
Only three witnesses were examined,
when an adjournment was made til' to
day. The witnesses were Henry Froe
li.it. bookmaker; S. W. Chubbuck, Oak
land, and Edward Ranfuraw, 2S3S Fol
som street; but their evidence was unim
portant. Foster had to give new bonds
yesterday, as it was discovered that the
ones he was released on were defective.

ISMMiiifYwEDUCATORS IN
ANNUAL SESSION
Teachers' Institute in Mission
High School.
President Bergerot of the Directors and
Mayor PheSan Deliver Addresses.
Work of Sections.
THE annual session of the Teachers' i
Institute of the city and county of
San Francisco began at 9 o'clock yes- j
terday morning In the auditorium of
the Mission High School. Every Beat was
taken and among the prominent persons
who occupied seats on the stage and par
ticipated in the exercises were President
Bergerot of the Board of Education, May
or Phelan and Prof. - Dresslar of ihe
University of California, The hall was
artistically decorated with pink and blue
bunting, national flags and flowers and j
ferns. President Bergerot in his address
gave the teachers a gentle hint that they
must keep up with the progressive spirit
Of the age if they hoped to retain their
positions; as mossbackism would not be
tolerated in the department.
The general assemblies are held morn- j
ings and evenings, the evenings being de
voted to section work divided among the
several grades. An interesting and "'"
structive illustrated lecture was delivered
in the Girls' High School last evening by
Professor J. Liberty Tadd of Pennsylva
nia on "New Methods in Education."
As a preliminary to the addresses it the
assembling of the teachers in the morn
ing the children of the fifth and , th
grades, under the direction of Miss Es
telle Carpenter, marched on the stage and
sang several pretty selections. Superin
tendent of Schools R. H. Webster, ex
officio president of the institute, then wel
comed the teachers and briefly outlined
the work to be done.
"It is ; , matter of gratification to know
that the movement represented in this
meeting has met with so much sympathy
from the leaders In education," said the
speaker, "and I must express my thanks
to the Board of Education and Its presi
dent who Is With us here to-day, to Pres
ident Jordan of Stanford University and
to the faculty of the University of Cali
fornia for their sympathy and support."
President Webster then introduced
President Bergerot of the Board of Edu
cation, who made a short address, in
which he said he believed that San Fran
cisco was entering upon a new era in edu
cational and municipal matters and ex
pressed the belief that with the new char
ter the best interests of the people would
be protected. Continuing he said:
"Our Freeholders deserve the plaudits
and the gratitude of the people of San
Francisco, and this is particularly true
In regard to the public school, which, In
spite of its delects, 18 representative of
tne best traits of American character.
Uur public school system is the bulwark
of our civilization, and there is no sacri
fice that we are not willing to make to
maintain its integrity. But our public
school system is yet in its infancy, and
what does not progress retrogrades. The
baneful influence of politics has tended to
lower the tone of our public schools, but
I am happy to say that this evil tendency
is in a fair way of being eliminated.
"Some of our teachers execute their
work, In a perfunctory' manner, as if the
community owed them a livipg. There
are others who do not keep in touch with
the best literature Of their profession.
Under the charter such teachers as do
not come up to the standard will be elim
inated. The new Board of Education is
animated with the termination to place
the public schools on a Bound, economical,
financial basis. We hope you will co-op
erate with us In bringing order out of
chaos."
Mayor Phelan was then introduced and
spoke on the "Importance of the Perma.
nent Improvement of Our Public School
Department." He referred to the dis
graceful record of the last board, and
said it was a matter of congratulation
that the city had been able to secure such
a good and efficient board as the present
one. *
A city offers a great many induce-,
ments for a population to remain within
Its limits," continued the Mayor, "if it
permanently improves i's educational
system. We need not fear the loss of our
pre-eminence In this respect, for San
Francisco is a natural educational center.
For the sparse population of California
our two great universities are remarkable
and these educational institutions at our
door furnish a strong guarantee of perma
nent improvement In our school system.
"We have accomplished much in the
new charter, under which a fund will be
created so that the teachers will be sure
of their salaries at the end of every cal
endar month, and the charter will also
give us the means of providing new build
ings. In this connection I desire to say
that If there is one thing that the last
board did for the city that is not a mat
ter of regret it is in giving us this mag
nificent building."
The Mayor referred to the necessity of
looking after the health of the pupils In
the public schools. He regarded that as
of even more Importance than the educa
tion of the youth, and in conclusion ho
felicitated the teachers on the municipal
legislation which he declared would re
sult In lasting benefit to the school sys
tem of the city.
At the conclusion of the Mayor's ad
dress a recess of five minutes was taken,
and upon reconvening a vocal solo was
very pleasingly rendered by Mrs. C. W.
Mark. Dr. F. B. Dresslar of the Depart
ment of Pedagogy of the University of
California was then Introduced to deliver
an address on "Hygiene In Public
Schools." He said In part:
" 'A good mind In a good body,' it is
said. 1 will go further and say. 'Good
morals In a good body." While I am a
thorough believer In education, I believe
more In good health, and if we can help
the children to acquire a good education
in a healthful way we are doing a great
deal for the State. We need better air.
If we want pure air we must continually
change the air in the school rooms so it
won't create a draught. We hear a great
deal about children being overworked. A
certain amount of fatigue Is necessary
and healthful. Merely . being awake and
sitting up produces a certain amount of
fatigue. : - : :.-'
"We want strong, healthful, vigorous,
quiet, determined people as teachers,
because we as Americans particularly
r.ecd It. If we are going to be of the
most service to ourselves, teachers, let us
take care of our health. Let us take
plenty of sleep, as this is the only real
method of relaxation.
"Children in the upper grades often get
too little sleep. They have too many par
ties'at home. If children do school work
properly they haven't much time for any
thing else.
"Variety cases fatigue. Recitations
should be changed frequently, but not too
frequently, because the other extreme of
too much variety tires, too.
"Two-Hevenths of the height of a boy
Should bf the heiÃht of his desk. Very
few children In the State are properly

A Wayward Daughter.
Mrs. Edna Aubray. while walking along
Golden Gate avenue yesterday after
noon with her little girl, was arrested by-
Secretary Wadham of the Eureka So
ciety for the Prevention of Cruelty to
Children and taken to the City Prison,
where a charge of vagrancy was booked
against her. Her little girl, 2% years of
age was booked for a public institution.
The : plaining witness against Mrs.
Aubray is her father, Captain Van Pelt
of the steamer Ukiah.
A suit was Instituted for divorce by
Mrs. Aubray from her husband, Charles
Aubray, a teamster, but 11 was with
drawn and fhey went to live together
again, but Saturday Mrs. Aubray left
him and no one knew of her whereabouts
till yesterday afternoon. Her father al
leges that she is not a fit person to have
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, APRIL 3, 1899.
the custody of the child, and as she in
tended leaving the city with her child her
arrest followed. . /: .
BAY CITY CLUB'S
VAUDEVILLE SHOW
FINE PROGRAMME OF SELECTED
TALENT.
Wheelmen and Their Friends Will
Crowd Native Sons' Hall To-
Night Dancing Will
Follow.
The Bay City Wheelmen will hold their
annual vaudeville entertainment thiS
evening at Native Sons' Hall, 414 Mason
street. The show will commence prompt
ly at 8 o'clock, and there will be dancing
at 10:30. A programme of remarkable ex
cellence has been arranged, including
Miss Eleanor Glustl, the talented soprano,
late of the Bostonians and now with the
Southwell Opera Company, and three,
"turns" from the Orpheum. The rest of
the talent comprises the best local ama
teurs, and altogether a fine evening's en
tertainment is promised.
The programme in detail is as follows:
Billy Hynes, comic selections; Miss Mer
rill F. Dryfuss (kindness of Orpheum)'
ballad vocalist; Orr and Atkinson, in
their (shirt) specialties; Ben Tarbox,
barytone selections; Al Hazard, ventrilo
quist (kindness of Orpheum); Jack Des
mond, tenor; Polk and Kollins, banjoists
I (kindness of Orpheum); Miss Eleanor
I Glusti, soprano (by permission of Charles
| L. Southwell); Hother Wlsmer, violinist;
I George Kanzee and his anlmatoscope.
j After the entertainment dancing will be
I indulged in until. midnight. Judge Frank
| H. Kerrigan, Henry L. Day, Archie Reid
and other prominent members of the club
! are managing the affair.
Reserved seats will be obtainable to
day at Sherman. Clay & Co.'s music store,
and this evening at the hall. The advance
sale has been large and a big crowd is ex
pected.
CLARK'S DEFENSE OPENS.
Experts Will Be Called to Testify to
His Mental Condition.
The case of Joseph Clark, now on trial I
for the murder of Joseph P. Gross in No- !
vember last, again occupied the attention j
of Judge Dunne's court J-esterday. The j
case of the people was completed Boon i
after midday, and then H. Digby John
ston, an English barrister and formerly
a member of the English House of Com
mons, senior counsel* for the defendant, !
opened his case, occupying the remainder
of the sitting with the story of the de
fendant's life and the circumstances
which resulted in the homicide.
At the conclusion of the opening argu
ment the case went over till this morn
ing. At the opening of court to-day wit- !
nesses for the defense will take the stand
and give their views of the killing. Louis
P. Boardman, who, with Mr. Johnston, is i
handling defendant's case, is building the
technical end of the fight. Witnesses to j
testify as to the conditions which might
and as a rule produce temporary aberra
tion of the mind will be called by the 1
defense. Among these Dr. S. Bishop will ;
take the stand. For a period of years
Dr. Bishop was the resident physician of
the Nevada Insane Asylum, and is an au
thority on diseases of the mind, the
causes of such diseases and their effect.
Other physicians of standing will be
called, and even though Clark's liberty
is sacrificed the defense is confident that
his neck can be saved the pain of frac
ture on the gallows.

made recently to the buildings in
the manufacturing district south

ture has been built by the Pelton Wa
ter Wheel Company to replace the one
occupied by them during the past ten
years. It Is on the east side of Main
street, between Mission and Howard
streets. As shown in the illustration.
It is a four-story, structure, with an
ornamental front of buff brick. It cov
ers a lot 68% feet by 137% feet, | and
provides 20,000 feet of floor space, all
to be used by the Pelton Company.
The main floor will be given over to
show rooms and the manufacturing
department. The latter will be brill
iantly lighted, the roof being com
posed almost completely of glass. The
second floor will be given over to the
SUPERVISORS WILL
GRANT THE PETITION
Mutual Electric Light Company
May Erect Their Poles.
A Majority of the Board Are Said to Be
Now in Favor of Helping the.
New Company.
THE war against the overhead wire j
systems of the electric light and I
telephone monopolies was warmly j
waged yesterday afternoon before j
; the Street Committee of the Board of j
I Supervisors, and it developed an intima- '
{ tion of what ultimate disposition would ;
! be made by the majority of the board of
the petition of the Mutual Electric Light
| Company to be allowed to erect poles out- j
j side of the fire limits. The resolution I
| praying for this privilege was lost at the }
! last meeting of the board, but Supervisor j
! Lackmann saved It from absolute down
fall by changing his vote from aye to no
and moving a reconsideration.
When the resolution Is brought up in '
its regular order at next Monday's meet- !
ing of the board it will be amended j
', slightly by Supervisor Aigeltinger and in j
; all probability passed by the board. In fact ]
1 it is almost a certainty that the majority |
I of the Supervisors will vote to grant the â 
; Mutual Electric Light Company permis- !
j sion to erect and maintain poles and an !
overhead wire system outside of the fire j
| limits. Supervisor Aigeltinger's amend- '
| ment will' not affect the general Import j
of the resolution. It simply makes It in- j
I cumbent upon the Mutual Electric Light j
Company to share Its poles with all other
competing light companies whether in ex- j
istence now or those which may come to I
life after the granting of the privilege.
j The Supervisors feel that they are
justified in placing this obligation on the
i Mutual Electric Light Company in order i
j that they will not be compelled to issue
| privileges of the same kind to other com- I
! peting companies, thus disfiguring the I
beauty of the outside districts. Supervi-
I sor Aigeltinger stated yesterday that he i
was almost absolutely certain that a ma- |
jority of the board would support his
amendment when the matter was brought
up at the next meeting of the board.
The inquiry regarding the attitude of
the various telegraph, telephone and elec
tric light and power companies on the
proposition to compel them to place all
their wires in the downtown districts un
derground was particularly interesting,
inasmuch as representatives of all the
lighting, telephone and electric companies
were present.
F. W. Dohrmann, president of the Mer
chants' Association and others interested
in the advancement of the city's interests
were present at the meeting. Mr. Dohr
mann addressed the committee on behalf
! of the Merchants' Association and made
several suggestions, the adoption of any
j one of which would lead the board out of
j the present difficulty.
"We believe that the various corpora
tions operating overhead wire systems
should be ultimately compelled to place
their wires underground, but we believe
j In giving the companies sufficient time
jln which to accomplish the change. I j
suggest that we all meet some time next
week and discuss In an informal manner
and in good faith a plan whereby this j
can be successfully accomplished. Mayor i
I Phelan'B plan of municipal ownership of
I one line or system of poles in the outside i
general offices and draughting' rooms,
while the third and fourth floors will
contain the pattern shops and pattern
store rooms. yi ; y-^yyy
At present the building seems out of
place in its dingy surroundings. On
all sides the buildings are of old style
and are marked with the smoke from
foundries and mills. In the new era of
prosperity which authorities on com
merce predict is approaching this is

"Do you believe, as the representative
of the Merchants' Association, that mu
nicipal ownership of electric light and
telephone poles in the outside districts
would solve this problem?" asked the
Mayor. â¢ â¢
"1 know nothing of the technical nature
of the proposition new before us," return
ed Mr. Dohrmann, "but speaking of It In
its general nature I should think It would.
I see no reason why the light, power and
telephone and telegraph companies should
object to placing their wires underground
as far as possible within the fire limits,
but I believe in giving them sufficient time
in which to effect the change. Overhead
wires arc- a menace to firemen in the per
formance of their duty, and it is time
some steps were taken to place them un
derground."
President Crockett of the San Francisco
Gas .and Electric Company was called
upon and he stated that his company
was placing its wires underground as far j
as possible in the downtown districts, and
had been doing so wherever it had an op
portunity during the last three years. Mr.
Crockett, In answer to a question put to
him by the Mayor, stated that he could
not estimate the length of time which
would necessarily have to elapse before
all wires could be placed underground.
Mr. Green, who represents the electric
light branch of Crockett's company, stat
ed that If the board ordered the company
to place all its wires underground it would
be compelled to abolish its Townsend
street plant. . f-;:^,. '.;'â ..;.; .;',v:
, "Why would that step be necessary?"
asked Supervisor Holland.
"Well," said Green, "we would have to
abolish fully a thousand arc lights now
operated by an overhead current. New
lights would have to be purchased and
erected, and we would be compelled to fit
up this plant for an underground sys
tem." ,?'i'i--.
Louis Glass, general manager of the Pa
cific Telephone and Telegraph monopoly,
was called upon by the committee to fur
nish information concerning the overhead
telephone system, and on the possibility
of compelling his company to share the j
same poles with the light and power com
panies in the event of the municipal own
ership idea being adopted by the board.
"It would be practically impossible," he
said, "for our company to string our
wires on the same poles as the light or
power companies. It would mean ruin to
both our service and our system. Joint
occupation of poles is simply out of the
question. The induction when we run
parallel to light or power lines is strong
enough to be dangerous to our service
and patrons. Fully 80 per cent of our
telephone lines have been placed under
ground, and It is only in places where
we are compelled to run our lines up on
the side or over the top of frame build
ings that we use poles within the fire
limits. The distribution of our current
and wires is the difficult problem that
we have to contend with."
L. D. Storror, representing the Postal
Telegraph Company, and Frank Jaynes,
superintendent of the Western Union Tel
egraph Company, both appeared before
the committee and expressed a willingness
to place their wires underground at any
time or under any conditions which the
board saw fit to impose. -
"All we want is a reasonable length of
time in which to make the change, said
Mr. Jaynes in accommodating tones.

"We have only one pole In the fire lim

The peals of laughter which followed

shake of his head left little doubt as to

W. R. Hewitt, superintendent of the
fire alarm and police telegraph system,
was called upon to answer several ques
tions in relation to the technical portion
of the matter in hand.- Mr. Hewitt stated
that though the telephone wire may not
be safe when placed on the same pole
as a light or power wire, it would be ab
solutely safe If run in the same under
ground conduit. y_ Â
"There is absolutely no reason, he said,
"why the telephone wire cannot be placed
on the same underground wire conduit
as the light or power wire. The city can
erect a pole system outside the fire limits
for about $115,000. If such a system was
erected it could be so arranged that the
light and power wires could be strung
on one set of poles and the telephone and
police and fire alarm wires on another.
After a long discussion on matters per- |
taining to the municipal ownership of the
overhead wire system and the ultimate
ownership of the telephone and electric
light and power plants the meeting ad
journed, the representatives of the vari
ous companies interested agreeing to meet
with the Merchants' Association next
Monday at the rooms of the association
In the Mills building. W. R. Hewitt was
instructed by Mayor Phelan to be present
at the meeting and report to the commit- i
tee all suggestions and statements made j
by those interested in the discussion.

Kicked Him to Death.
John Shaughnessy is on trial before
Judge Cook on a charge of manslaughter..
On October 4 last he killed a man name-
F. Keys during a fight. The evidence
develops the fact, that after knocking
Keys down. Shaughnessy kicked him se
verely, from the effects of which he died.

LAST FRIDAY'S BREAK.
The New York circular of Henry Clews, writ
ten the day after the break in industrials last
week, says: "The market is in a shape from
which wide fluctuations may be expected. Yes
terday we had an illustration of this in a sharp
breakdown of a certain group of Industrials,
which extended its effects through the general
list. That break should have come during the
latter part of March, as I prognosticated it
would, but there was united action to stem the
current at that time by a number of powerful
leaders with a view of bridging over the Ist
of April settlements, expecting easy money
thereafter. Their calculations as to the speedy
return of money to this center having mis
carried, they lost their grip in holding the
market and in the severity of the raids which
caught an innumerable number of stop orders
and weakly margined accounts. To the deluge
of stocks from this cause Is due the bad break.
It Is a very healthy reaction and not any too
severe considering the high prices that most
properties had attained. The recession in
prices now makes a new basis for an early
upward spring movement and Insures active
trading during the summer months which but
for the marking-down process would not have
been likely, for to have gone Into a bull sum
mer campaign on the high range of prices that
had been reached would have been like build
ing a mountain on the apex of another one.
"The firmness of the money market is to be
regarded as, on the whole, a healthy symptom,
as it is an evidence of business activity. Be
fore the expiration of this month, certain
operations requiring large transfers of funds,
however, will be out of the way; after which
there Is nothing ln sight to prevent a steady
and comparatively easy state of the loan
market until next fall."

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
Partly cloudy weather prevails over the coun
try west of the Rocky Mountains. Light rain
has fallen over Washington, Oregon. Idaho
and Southern Arizona.
The pressure is highest off the Washington
coast and lowest in Southern Nevada. It Is
rising rapidly over the Pacific Slope.
The temperature has fallen over Washington
and Oregon, and remained about stationary
elsewhere west of the Rocky Mountains.
Conditions are favorable for fair, warmer
weather In California Thursday.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, April 13:
Northern Californiaâ Fair Thursday; some
what warmer; fresh northwest wind.
Southern Californiaâ Partly cloudy Thursday;
fresh west wind.
â¢Nevadaâ Partly cloudy Thursday; cooler.
Utahâ Partly cloudy Thursday, probably with
showers In north portion; cooler.
Arizonaâ Partly cloudy Thursday, with show
ers ln north portion.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Thursday;
Warmer; fresh northwest wind.
G. H. WILLSON, Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. April There was a wild
rush to buy at the opening, and the specialties
made large gains, part of which they retained,
while the railways, which had improved smart
ly, left off at about yesterday's figures. The
rise of Americans in London before the open
ing and the momentum of yesterday was re
sponsible for the early excitement In the mar
ket. Call money ranged from 4 to 5, closing
at the lower figure, and sterling exchange was
strong throughout, demand sterling rising %.
The rapidity of the early advance Induced
heavy realisations, but a general tone of
strength' prevailed until the afternoon, when
some sharp recessions occurred. The killing
of American and /English navy officers In
Samoa seemed to arouse apprehension in some
quarters of further complications. Among the
specialties strong features were Brooklyn
Transit, Consolidated Gas, People's Gas,
Sugar and the iron and steel shares. Balti
more and Ohio Issues, Norfolk and Western
preferred and Northern Pacific were strong.
Foreign houses were early purchasers but re
sold about half as much on the local rise.
Some of the foreign purchases were connected
with London's fortnightly settlement. A
number of shorts were driven to cover early,
owing to the commission house demand, es
pecially for Industrials, because of a more lib
eral position taken by a number or banks
toward such stocks. The market closed steady.
There was more activity in railroad bonds to
day, and while a few Issues were shaded,
the general average was slightly better on the
day. Total sales, $3,760,000. United States 3s
advanced % In the bid price.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, April 12â The Commercial Ad- I
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were good early to-day. i
New York Central was the feature. New York
accepted London's level, and then there was a :
sharp relapse. Later New York bought North
ern Pacific, causing a general rally on the
street. The best Issues were St. Paul and
Norfolk and Western preferred.
Tlntos were 12. Other copper shares were |
dull.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET. â¢
CHICAGO, April 12.â Reports of crop condi
tions were the chief considerations again with
wheat speculators at the opening, and those
being of unvarying tone of badness the mar
ket opened strong. Receipts here and in the
Northwest were small, Chicago getting only
thirty-five cars, of which two were graded con
tract, and Minneapolis and Duluth 188 cars,
compared with 142 a year ago. The Missouri
Weather Bureau report of wheat conditions in
that State said many correspondents reported
half the wheat killed, and the lowa State re-
I port claimed late sown wheat was about all
dead and early sown seriously damaged.
Private reports were almost unanimously of a
most discouraging character with regard to the
Injury done. Those were not repetitions of
previous reports, but from fresh sources. May
opened %Â®%c higher at 71%Â®71%c. and dur
ing the first two hours rose to 72% c. At that
point, however, realizing commenced and
forced the price back again to 71%@71%c.
Damage claims again became popular, how
ever, and were given the most consideration.
There were several ups and downs as the ses
/ sion progressed, with trade very light. On
one of the downward reactions about forty
minutes from the close some heavy shorts at
tempted to cover. Immediately the price shot
up. May touching 72% c. closing with sellers at
72%Â®72%c.
There were a number of considerations In
corn and the market ruled firm. May closed
%c higher.
There was little doing in the oats market
and outside of an occasional spurt with wheat
it was very slow. May oats closed %c higher.
Provisions were very heavy. May liquida
tion was still under way and the course of
prices was controlled by that and covering by

THE BOSTON WOOL MARKET.
BOSTON, April 12.â The American Wool and
Cotton Reporter will say to-morrow:
Some of the large woolen mills, including
both those which are in the combine, have
been represented in the market during the j
past week and have purchased very liberally
of wool. The Washington, Wanskuck ami
Pacific mills have been especially prominent \
and they have taken some very large lines :
of territories, Oregon % and %s blood j
fleeces. The sales of territories alone aggre- !
gate over 4,000,000 pounds, which, combined
with those of other lines, make a total busi- j
ness. for the week of nearly 8,000,000 pounds.
Th.-re Is no change to note in prices, how
ever. The sales of the week in Boston amount
ed to 7,461,000 pounds domestic and 466.500
pounds foreign, making a total of 7,927,500,
against a total of 4.263,800 for the previous
week and a total of 530.530 -for the correspond
ing week last year. Sales since January 1,
1899. amount to 57,003.600 pounds, against 40,
--727,810 pounds last year at this time.

The Hay market has gone to the dogs. Al
though receipts were not as large yesterday
"here was an accumulation of , stock to be
worked off, and as buyers were shy the market
was demoralized. The flood from the farmers
has been too great. Outside Hay Is selling be
low the cost of laying it down here, as the
freight is 35 per ton. .

BUTTER, CHEESE AND EGGS.
Butter is ln larger supply and weak, though
there Is not much decline.
Eggs are lower and dull at the decline, with
accumulating stocks. The expected arrivals
from the East are the sole cause of the cur
rent depression.
