DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Reports from the coming crop are coming in
every day now. and while It Is too early for
any definite statement it may be said that
from indications up to date Peaches will be
the only heavy crop, Apricots will be slim,
Pears are not showing up well on the river
and Cherries and Prunes are dropping more
or less, owing to the dryness of the ground
last year. This Is the state of the crop at
present, but H is liable to change any day.
Receipts were 22 boxes Cherries and 37
chests Strawberries.
The market Is well supplied with Oranges,
but they are meeting with a good demand
and are steady.

Mail advices from New York say of Prunes: i
"Prunes are tendtnz upward because of en- j
larged demand and the close concentration of
stocks here and in California. Total holdings
on the coast will scarcely exceed 200 cars, and
one firm Is said to control the bulk of them.
The same condition exists here, consequently
advances are expected on all sizes and quali
ties. At present demand appears to be re
stricted, though there Is strong Inquiry, and
some out-of-town dealers are seeking supplies
to carry them over the spring activity before
there Is another advance. The outlook Is hope
ful for holders, but at the moment actual busi
ness Is comparatively light."

THE STOCK MARKET.
The sugar stocks were about the same on
the morning session of the Bond Exchange.
The light stocks were unsettled. Gas and
Electric declining to $74 and Mutual Electric
advancing to $15 75.
On the afternoon call business was more
active. Gas and Electric sold still lower at
$73 37%, Mutual Electric advanced to $16 12%
and Contra Costa Water to $65. Market-street
Railway declined to $62 75.
Mining stocks continued firm but quiet The
telegram from the pump said: "Elevator
'working steadily. Water In 1750 level winze
19 feet 7 inches below the station."
The semi-annual Interest on the bonds of the
Sierra Valley Railway Company is now payable
at the Crocker- Woolworth National Bank.
The following changes in the officials of the
Nevada National Bank are reported: George
Grant, cashier, In place of D. B. Davidson,
retired on account of ill health, and W. G.
McGavln, assistant cashier, in place of Mr.
Grant, promoted. The offlce of second vice
president has been created, and I. W. Hellman
has been appointed to the place.

Brown and the Twins.
"Yes." said Green, "I always thought
well of Brown until last night."
"What did he do last night?" asked
White. : .â¢;
"Well, you know," the first went on,
"we've boys at our houses, two of 'em,
twins, and as fine a pair of youngsters as
mortal man ever laid his eyes on. They're
bouncing fellows, and for the past week
all the neighbors have been in to see
them. Most of the girls Gertrude knows
have given the babies something, and
some of the men have sent over little
trifles like shaving mugs and cigar cases
and the like, to be saved for the boys un
til they're old enough to use them. We
expected a nice present from Brown and
his wife, for both Gertrude and I knew
them well before we all up and married.
Of course Brown was told of the twins
shortly after their arrival in this vale
of tears, and every evening we expected
he and Mrs. Brown would be over. They
came last night. Brown wanted to see
the boys, yet as they have never had any
little folks in his house, he hardly know
what to say. They were brought out by
the nurse, and he took one on each knee
holding them close to him, with his arms
around them, you know. Well, he" look
ed at them, blushed a little, appeared
rather foolish, and blurted out, "Brothers,
I presume? What do you think of that?"
âDetroit Free Press.

Portland, Or., April 10. 1899.
WRANGEL STRAIT, ALASKA.

Buoy No. 11, a black second class Iron spar,

Tar Hill Left the Maiden â Ros
inante First in Handicap Paul

by Headwater. : r
The mile and p. Quarter event on the
card at Oakland yesterday was a "feeler"
for th* Derby. There were four starters,
with Corrigan's Corsine a pronounced fa
vorite. The surprise stable of E. J. Bald
win also started one that had been pick
ling in the brine for some timeâ Los Me
dar.es. On April 7 the brown colt started
Jn a mile and -a sixteenth race raptured by
Joe Ull man, and while showing speed at
one stacre of tho journey was beaten by
the winner more than a dozen lengths.
The members of the ring chalked up 10 to
1 against him, and even the silverites
passed him up. He made all the running
and won from the favorite under restraint
in 2:08%. The winner is a good looking
piece of racing material, and should be
hard to beat on Saturday. Oiir.thus was
also a starter, but Shields choked him to
death while trying to rate tho chestnut
colt, and he ran last.
The attendance continues to grow
lighter every dny. Three favorites were
beaten
oeaien.
Corrigan won the Olympic stake at four
and a half furlongs with Golden Rule.
Winyah was not trying, and Burdoc took
second place from Moana.
The mile and a furlong handicap was
the one interesting feature of the after
noon. Rosinante and Topmast ruled equal
choices In the betting, and ln a drive Jen
kins landed the former winner by a head,
Plan finished at the saddle girths of Top
mast, but was disqualified and the show
given to David Tenny.
The knowing ones all put the checks
down on Billy Randall's entry Tulle i
in the opening mile and a sixteenth run.
Fending him to the post favorite. Show
ing the way for a mile the Montana horse
then tired and was passed by Peter
Weber, an S4-pounder, ridden by See,
which won easily. The winner was 4 to 1
in the betting.
Corrigan's Artillery filly Art ilia was the
redhot one in the "kindergarten" scram
ble, but a straggling start contributed to
her defeat, and the young miss finish*
third. Tar Hill, with Macklln tip and
once 7 to 1 in the ring, led throughout.
winning hands down from Flush of Gold.
The gray horse Paul Griggs was downed
in the six furlong dash by Headwater.
The filly closed favorite over him. and
though he made a stubborn chase after
her was led out over a length in 1:13%:
Guilder, unable to stand the hot clip set,
finished a fair third.

Trans-Mississippi Congress.
The Chamber of Commerce is in receipt !
; of a communication requesting the ap- ;
pointment of delegates to the Trans-Mis- j
sissippi Commercial Congress that is to j
meet in Wichita, Kan., in the latter rart |
of May. By the decision of the last con- j
vention San Francisco Is entitled to ten '
delegates and the State is entitled to a !
like representation, to be appointed by :
the. Governor. Every city will be repre- ;
sented. At the next meeting of the Cham- I
bet- Its delegates will be selected. When I
the congress convenes there will be many â â 
interesting subjects discussed. Prominent
among them will be "Irrigation and Arid
Regions," "improvement of Western j
Rivers," "Water Transportation." "Our ,
Trade and How to Increase it," "Agricul
ture," "Mining in the West," and special
subjects relating to "National Quaran
tine." "Statehood for Territories," "Mon- '
roe Doctrine." "Homestead Laws," "Our 1
Foreign Possessions." "Election of United
States Senators by the People." "Paris
Exposition" and "Trusts and Combines." >
Ellen M. Henrotin, ex-president of
the Federated Woman's Clubs of
America, writes about the woman's
clubs of California, in next Sunday's
Call.
An Appeal for Books.
Mrs. "W. B. Harrington, president of
the California Red Cross Society, makes
an appeal for books and magazines to be
used at the Red Cross reading-room at
the Presidio. The arrival of hundreds of
recruits makes this demand for reading
matter imperative, and the society will
send for any, if notified at the "office,
room 309, Examiner building.

Battered His Wife.
Mrs. Rigeno Brown, 721 Brannan street,
swore to a complaint In Judge Mogan's
court yesterday for the arrest of licr
husband, Jacob Brown, a baker, on the
charge of battery. Sunday night while
drunk he struck her several times and
also blackened his little girl's eyes.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, APRIL 13, 18 _&.
SACRAMENTO IS
INSTALLED IN
FIRST POSITION
Both Protests Are
Thrown Out.
LEAGUE DIRECTORS MEET
LOCHHEAD OF THE CAPITAL
CTTYS TO ST. LOUIS.
Umpire Creamer Released and O'Con
nor Appointed to Succeed Him.
Oakland Is in Hard
Luck.
The directors of the California League
at a meeting last night voted to disallow
i the protest of San Francisco against the
! game with Sacramento on April 1, and
| that of Santa Cruz against Sacramento
; in the game of April 8, on account of the
I playing of Hughes, a National League
: player, by that team. There were pres
| ent at the meeting President Moran. Vice
President Bert, Secretary Bonnet, Mana
! gers Harris of San Francisco, Kripp of
! Sacramento, Struve of Watsonville,
| Borchers of San Jose and Swing of. Oak
; land. Secretary Bonnet represented
\ Santa Cruz by proxy.
When the matter of the protested
! games was taken up, Manager Kripp i
said he was not aware of the California i
| League's membership in the national I
j agreement, as he had not been officially I
. notified, a statement that was corrobo- !
i rated- by the president and secretary,
who admitted they had failed to send
! out the notification.
Harris said he filed his protest to es- j
i tablish a precedent and to systemize the j
affairs of the league, and not because he |
wanted any games he had not fairly won.
Upon this, both protests were thrown out
by the board of arbitration, consisting of
I the president, vice president and secre-
I tary. Manager Kripp's action in playing
Hughes merited severe precautionary
'. measures against a repetition. His ex
cuse, that he did not know the league was
i under the national agreement, will hard
{ ly hold, in the Santa Cruz protest, at any
rate, for Manager Harris' protest the
week before J ought to have been suffi
cient notice ' to him. Yet he played
Hughes the following Saturday, and
; would in all probability be playing htm
vet had Hughes not departed for the
East.
However, the board took steps to pre
vent a recurrence of the trouble by
i adopting a rule that any club using an
I ineligible player shall forfeit the game
and be fined $100 as well.
President Moran announced the release
; of Umpire Creamer, and the appointment
of Mr. O'Gonnell of San Francisco in his
i place. Creamer has caused general dis
satisfaction by his work.
The positions of the league teams have,
been changed considerably since a week
I ago. By winning two straight from
Santa Cruz Sacramento goes to the top,
and the Fresno team, by defeating Wat
' sonville twice, takes second place, send
: ing Watsonville, who led a week ago, to
I fifth place. Oakland and San Jose are
! tied for third place, and Santa Cruz
brings up the rear. The percentage is aa .

San Jose is bound to show some marks
in Its error column whenever Fatty Klei
ner is In the game, for the big fellow
never shirks anything that comes his
way. He handles many drives that would
be safe hits with anybody else in the po
sition, but of course there are some that
he cannot handle cleanly. It is surpris
ing how easily he gets BO much avoirdu
pois over the ground.
Santa Cruz is smashing the ball as hard
as any of them this season, but does not
seem to have settled down into its regu
lar fielding gait yet. Unseemly errors
have been responsible for most of its de
feats so far. The boys were handicapped
at the start, however, by having their
grounds converted into an ideal duck
pond just when they wanted to begin
practice. With the grounds in good con
dition again, the Crabs will soon be In it
.with the others.
Oakland Is the "hard luck" team of the
league this season. Losing three of its
best men In two days is enough to worry
any manager, but (Swing is not discour
aged, and announces that he will take
I both games from San Jose this week..
It is expected that Hammond will be in
I condition to catch for the Oaklands this
' week. Dunleavy and Donovan, however,
i wi-il not be in shape to play for some time.
The San Jose team play the Stanford
1 College team a game of hall every day
I this week, thereby giving both teams the
best kind of practice.
There appears to be two cliques in the
Watsonville team, each seeking the con
trol of the team, and as a consequence
; the chances of the club are greatly in-
I jured by not getting the best efforts of the
; players.
George Harper and Ham Tberg will be
I the opposing twirlers In Sunday's game.
! This ought to insure a splendid exhibition
I of the pitcher's art, as both players are
! complete masters of scientific pitching.
In the contest at Watsonville last Sun
day neither team made th.- semblance of
an error until the ninth inning, when the
- Watsonville catcher made an error on a
I thrown ball to second base.
The games this week will be as follows-
Watsonville comes to San Francisco, Oak
! land goes to San Jose and Sacramento
I will play at Santa Cruz.

SACRAMENTO, April 12.â The game
has caught the capital city and no mis
take. More interest has been manifested
so far this season than at any time since
the days when the old Alias dished, out a
quality of baseball that would make the
hair assume a perpendicular position.
Fully a thousand people witnessed the
game last Saturday between the Sacra
mentos and Santa Cruzans, and double
that number went out to Oak Park the
following day to see the same teams per
form. Many more would have attended
the Sunday game had the 'weather been
more pleasant. ;.je . â¢ ',â
The "cranks are "tickled to death at
the team's winning three out of Tour,
which puts them in front place. Last sea
son the Senators- started off in first place
and were never headed. Wonder if they
are going to repeat the dose? - -
"Jay" Hughes and Harry Loehhead left
for the East last Sunday night. Both are
to play in the major leagueâ the former is
on Brooklyn's pitching staff, while the
latter will cover shortstop for St. Louis.
Lochhead's departure was a surprise even
to his most intimate friends. It was
known he signed with St. Louis late last
fall, but owing to the fact that the Cleve
land team was switched to St. Louis it
was thought "Loch's" chances of going to
St. Louis had gone a-gllmmering. About
three weeks ago, however, a ticket and a
piece of advance money came to the big i
shortstop and he wired back "Will come.
The loss of Loehhead is a great one, and
the team will feel it keenly. . Â».
Henry Stulz will in all probability be
signed to take Lochhead's place, but with
all due respect to Mr. Stulz we are sure
the contract will be too great to enable !
him to Jump into immediate favor with
the local "fans."
The local team has the distinction of be
ing the only team in California which .in
tho past three years has developed Na
tional Leaguers. The Senators manage
to turn out at least one each season, and
last season both Harvey and Doyle could
have signed with teams In the big league,
but they preferred to stay on the coast.
Pittsburg was hot after Harvey all
through last season, and he ignored tele
gram after telegram. Doyle could have
gone with Loehhead to St. Louis. Stan
ley, our crack catcher, can' play in the
East whenever he desires to, as several
teams are after this star backstop.
Warren W. Beckwith Jr., the new ac
quisition, is proving himself to be a great
ball player. He has Just arrived here from
Chicago, and is a great friend of Mat
Stanley, having played with him two sea
; sons in the Texas League. This same
j Warren Beckwith has had quite a little
romance. It will be remembered that not
I so very long ago there was an elopement
! in the family of Robert B. Lincoln, son
j of President Lincoln, which set the news
papers and gossips all over the country
i talking. Beckwith, who at that time had
i Just graduated from one of the big East-
I crn colleges, was a great football player
and general all-round athlete of no mean
I ability. He became infatuated with Rob
ert Lincoln's daughter, but her folks
would not hear of her marrying Beck
with, and the couple were obliged to elope.
This proceeding greatly angered the pa
rents of the young bride and they refused
to recognize the marriage, and further- \
more they disowned their daughter. After
I the elopement young Beckwith took his
wife to Chicago and he began playing pro
fessional ball. His profession took him
all over ball. countryâ to Missouri, to
over the countryâ to Missouri, to
Texas, to Colorado and finally to the
Golden Suite. He Intends settling down
now that the Llncolns have been recon
ciled, and intends to make California his
future home. His wife will Join him as'
soon as he "gets settled." She is at pres
ent with relatives in Denver, Colo.
Next Saturday and Sunday the team
plays in Santa Cruz. The boys will
make their first appearance in their new
uniforms, which arrived from the East
Monday. These uniforms are by far the
"swellest" in the league.
Tommy Sheehan, the little round fel
low, as he Is termed at home, is playing
the fastest ball Imaginable. He is surely
the "find" of the season. He has caught
on with the local "cranks" for keeps.
Beckwith may be put in to pitch one
of the games in Santa Cruz this week.
The lad has plenty of speed and good
curves. He has pitched before; in fact
that was his position while in the Chi
cago league. :;â¢-â¢;->-
Tim Shanahan still looks after the first
bag â¢in his same old style, and we might
add that he still retains that "same old
smile." 'â¢â â 'â¢ . â '. '.'-'â¢-.â 
"Kid" Peeples la playing a great third
for the Senators. He continues to prac
tice faithfully, and will soon work
around to his old-time form.
,The entire team practices for two solid
hours every day in the week but Monday
and the days they play, It is the faith,
ful practice that does as much as any
thing toward landing the pennant each
season.
RELIGION FOR THE YOUNG.
Sunday School Workers' Meeting in
the University Town to Discuss
the Plans of Their Work.- .
BERKELEY, April 12.â The delegates to |
the State Sunday-school Association met
this morning and afternoon in the First
Congregational Church on Dana street. A
fair attendance of Sunday-school teachers
from a"l! over the State made the disucs
slons quite exhaustive. Papers were read
upon the salient points connected with
religious teaching for the young. A ques
tion box added much to the interest of
the gathering and brought out new ideas
connected with this special branch of
work. V-* -
The convention will come to a close to
morrow noon. For the morning session
the order of exercises has been arranged
as follows:
Bible study, led by the Rev. W. C. Sher
man of Sacramento; address, "Hold That
Boy," Joseph I). Radford of San Jose;
address, "Temperance in the Sunday
school," Rev. EC. L. McCracken of San
Francisco; missionary talk, Mrs. W. C.
Sherman; report of State statistical sec
retary, Mrs. Nellie i). Harp of Stockton:
address, "House to House Visitation,"
by the general secretary; address, "Nor
mal Class Methods." 11. Morton of San
Jose; address. "The Consecration of
Childhood to Christ," Bishop Moreland of i
San Francisco.
Sullivan Must Go.
ALAMEDA, April 12.â 1t is now definite
ly stated that School Superintendent Sul
livan will be retired as soon as the new
Board of Education organizes. Not ono
of the Trustees is in favor of his reten
tion. Dr. Scott and Cyrus Brown, the
holdovers, both agree with Messrs. Keys,
Otis and Sloan, the new members, thai
the Interests of the department demand
Sullivan's dismissal. The gentlemen cau
cussed this evening at the home, of Trustee
it's. Those who were mentioned In con
nection with Sullivan's position are Pro
fessor Hughes of the Encinal School, Pro-*
fessor Scott of the High School and Pro
fessor Biedenboch, instructor In mathe
matics in the Oakland High School. Dr.
E. M. Keys will probably be elected chair
man of the new board. The salary of the
superintendent is J2OO a month. â¢

said a thimbleful will furnish enough
plants for an acre of ground.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
FORGET-ME-NOTS
PLACED ON TOE
MOTHER'S COFFIN
Funeral of the Late
Mrs. Yon Schmidt.
CHILDREN'S TOKEN OF LOVE
PATHETIC SCENES ATTEND THE
SERVICES.
Brother and Sister-in-Law of the De
ceased Were Overcome by Grief
and Had to Be Carried
From the Church.
Alameda Office San Francisco Call,
f!42S .Park street, April 12.
Rosa Alexis and Edwin yon Schmidt, the
eldest of the five children of the late
Captain and Mrs. E. A. yon Schmidt,
each placed a little bunch of forget-me
nots on their mother's coffin to-day and
tearfully bade a last farewell to the little
woman whose pleasure on earth had been
In caring for them.
The funeral services were held this
morning at the Unitarian church. The
scenes and incidents attending the last sad
rites over the earthly remains of the mur
dered woman were pathetic ln the ex
treme. The bereaved relatives present
were Major and Mrs. C. E. Tilden, the
three older childrenâ Rose, Alexis and
Edwinâ and Charles Hill and wife of
Denver, Col., brother and sister-in-law of
the deceased. Colonel A. W. yon Schmidt
Is prostrated at his home and could not
be present.
When Mr. Hill walked down the aisle
of the church and caught the first glimpse
of the flower-laden casket containing the
remains of his murdered Bister, he was
completely overcome with grief and
swooned before the bier. Tender hands
assisted him to a chair and friends tried
to persuade him to leave the church until
the trying scenes were over. He insisted,
however, Upon remaining through the ser
vices, lie had to be carried to the casket
to take a last look at his dead sister. His
cries and moaning, as he fell across the
coflin covering his sister's face with kisses
and his tears were agonizing. He had to
be almost torn away from the dead, and
when he turned from the casket ho fell
helpless Into the arms of his friends. His
wife also fainted during the services and
was carried from the church.
The three little children bore up bravely
under their burden of woe. Major Tllde.n
lifted each one up to the coffin, and as
they looked for the last time on their
mother and impressed a final kiss on her
stilled lips they cried as though their
hearts were breaking. Each, as gently
lifted away, divided a little bunch of for
get-me-nots carried, placing half in the
casket with "mamma" and clinging to the
remaining fading little flowers, significant
of that love and reverence which will bo
the guiding inspiration through life.
During the services Mrs. Flelssner-
Lewls and Miss Florence Young sang "So
Fades the Lovely Blooming Flower' and
"Abide With Me." Miss Young rendered
a contralto solo, "O Love Divine," by
Kevin.
The chancel and pulpit were a mass of
rosses, calla lilies, marguerites, palms
and ferns, and the bier was beautified
with roses of bite and pink.
Rev. G. R. Dodson, who conducted the
services, spoke in the following touching
manner of the deceased:
"My friends death gives a new per
spective to a human life. It causes the
unessential elements to drop out of sight
and leaves only the main outlines and
grand features of the character. I am
glad to unit-- with you now in paying a
tribute of respect to the life that was
so suddenly quenched In Sunday's trag
edy. It was the life of a woman, one of
Eve's daughters, a wife and a mbther,
an admirable character, irreproachable
and altogether worthy of the esteem and
love of her neighbors and family. While
it Is not my habit to make personal refer
ence on an occasion of this kind. I feel
it my privilege as a Citizen, now that she
Is gone and her sad story is becoming
known, to do her the justice to say that
Bhe stands cleared of every evil Insinua
tion ever made against her character.
"By those who have known her long
and intimately, and whose word I im
plicitly trust, she is described to me as
having been exceptionally brave and
cheerful in the trying circumstances
which were her earthly lot.
"Buoyant, courageous, unselfish, she
did her best. Though the mother of five
children, she still had some room in her
heart for other little ones who had no
one to love or care for them. Without
neglecting her own household she found
time, with other ladles, to establish In
our city a home for homeless children.
"Now her brief and eventful life Is over.
She has been torn away from her little
rood, ln loving and caring for whom
Ehe found her highest happiness.
"Poor, brave little woman! Even were
you alive now little could we help you,
and now that you are gone you are alto
gether beyond us. I thank God that good
ness dwells In the hearts of men and wo
men, and that other love will do its best
to supply the love of the mother's
heart. : . /
The pallbearers were: C. J. Lancaster,
Alexander Baum, J. W. Burnham and 1..
J. Healy. The remains were cremated
at Odd Fellows' Cemetery, San Fran
cisco.
The funeral of Captain yon Schmidt will
be held to-morrow afternoon from Pio
neer Hall. interment will be at Laurel
Hill Cemetery.
FIXING OF 6RANDES'
SENTENCE IS DELAYED
ATTORNEYS YET TOO WEARY TO
MOVE.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 12.
William A. Brandes, convicted of the j
murder of his little daughter, was not I
sentenced to-day, and his wife, Etta Bran
ded, also awaiting trial on a murder
charge, was not released on a writ of ha
beas corpus.
Both matters came up before Judge
Hall, but Attorney Sawyer asked for fur
ther time in which to arrange the fabric
upon which he will base his motion for a
new trial in Murderer Brandes' cause.
The attorney stated that "the court un
doubtedly realizes how tired the attorneys \
(were at the conclusion of the protracted :
trial," and he said also that he "did. not
have the heart to go over tho mass of
testimony," because It made him sick to
look at it. There being no objection on |
the part of the District Attorney the mat- 'â¢
ter went over for one week.
The case of Mrs. Etta Brandes likewise
went over for another week. Judge Hall
stated that he, too, had been tired and
had found little or no time to examine I
the testimony adduced at Mrs. Brandes* !
preliminary hearing: The accused mur
deress has now occupied her cell at the
Counts. Jail since last November, yet she j
appeared In court to-day in very cheerful i
spirits
Brandes' attorneys have applied to the
court for payment for the expert wit
nesses employed ln his defense. This ap
plication will perhaps end a contention
that has been going on in the Superior 1
Courts for many years.
There Is a statute which permits the '
payment of expert fees ln criminal cases
upon the signed order of a Judge. In the
trial of Clara Fallmer the matter was >
brought up by ex-Judge Frick. In this
case several experts on insanity were em- !
ployed on either side, those for the prose
cution receiving $50 apiece. Judge Frick â 
handed in bills for the payment of the ex
perts for the defense, but they were de
nied. Judge Ogden holding that the stat
ute required that the Judge should name
the experts If the State should pay for
them.
In the Brandes case the attorneys for
the defence BU_mmonedi.t_i_r.owa_eÂ«P_erta_

and now Attorney Bennett is asking that
the county pay their fees. Judge Hall
stated that he did not wish to establish
a precedent in this matter without con
sulting his brother Judges, and took the
matter under advisement.

1 j from the university and sailed with one
| j of the first expeditions to the Philippines.
He Is now home from the front on sick
leave, but expects to return within a few
i days. The college troops gave him a
â¢ : royal welcome as he appeared on the
. campus at drill hour. It required but a
signal from the two majors and both bat
| I tallons burst into three cheers for their
former officer. Major Cloman made a
. short address, thanking the students for
. j his reception. He spoke briefly of the
California students at Manila, paying a
. tribute to their bravery and efficiency. It
i was his wish, he said, to resume his work
. at Berkeley should the course be open
next summer. At the close of the address
i the two battalions marched to the lower
! campus, where they were formed for reg
â¢ j imental review. Major Cloman received
i the review in company with Professor

RECKLESS DRIVERS ARRESTED.
Mrs. A. B. Carlock and Policeman
Dennis Coleman Knocked Down
on Market Street.
W. Gillen, a driver for 1,. E. Clausen
Co., 1340 Market street, was arrested yes
terday afternoon by Policeman Ross on
warrants charging him with fast driving
and battery. He was released on his own
recognizance by Judge Conlan.
The complaining witness against him Is
A. B. Oarlock of Siskiyou County, who,
With his wife, is a guest at the Grand Ho
tel. He and his wife were crossing Mar
ket street at Montgomery yesterday
morning, when Gillen came driving along
at a rapid rate. Carlock held up his hand
for him to stop, but he kept on and Mrs.
Carlock was struck and knocked down.
She was carried to the hotel and a physi
cian summoned.
K. NeltO, an expressman, was also ar
rested yesterday afternoon on a charge
of battery by Policeman W. J. Smith. He
was driving across Third and Market
streets and ran into Policeman Dennis
Coleman, who was on duty at the cross
ing, knocking him down, but fortunately
not Injuring him. Policeman Smith hap
pened to be passing on a car at the time
and jumped off, placing Nelto under ar
rest.
Revolutionary Daughters.
A reception will be tendered by the Sons
of the American Revolution to the
Daughters of the American Revolution
at the California Hotel on the evening of
April 19 from 8 o'clock until 11. . Music
and refreshments are on the programme.
As this Is the first reception- tendered by
one of the societies to the other, a large
and fashionable gathering Is expected. A
large reception committee consisting of
twenty-five members has the affair in
preparation and will assist in receiving
the guests. The president of the Califor
nia Society of Sons of the Revolution is
Horace Davis, and among the regents of
the Revolutionary Daughters are Mrs. J.
F. Swift. State regent; Mrs. F. O. Moody,
Mrs. Henry Gibbons, Mrs. Henry Krebs,
Mrs. Henry Wetherbee, Mrs. W. R. Smed
berg, of San Francisco, and Mrs. G. W.
Percy of Oakland.
STRUGGLED TO PREVENT
HER MAMMA'S SUICIDE
Oakland Offlce San Francisco Call, 908 Broadway, April 12.
ff/""\H! mamma, please don't drink that nasty stuff!" cried little Ellen
J I Caswell as she saw her mother taking poison. The childish request
M was not heeded, the suppliant attempted to prevent by force her
mother's suicide, but in vain. The little arms-could not restrain the
will of a woman bent on self-destruction.
Mrs. Warren Caswell of the Homestead, near Hay wards, ended her life
this evening by taking a drink of carbolic acid. There was no one in the
house at the time but her 6-year-old daughter, and although help was sum
moned by the alarmed neighbors it was of no use, for Mrs. Caswell had done
her work too well. -,-â¢
The little daughter being the only witness was the only one who could
throw any light on the tragedy, and between her sobs she told a pitiful story
to the Deputy Coroner.
"When I saw mamma take the bottle with the red label on I knew she
must not drink it," she said. "Mamma told me never to touch that bottle,
for it would kill me if I drank any of It. I told her not to drink the nasty
stuff, but she did not seem to hear me. Then I ran up to her and tried to
take it out of her hands, hut I could not. I am not strong enough to take
anything from mamma. She just would drink It, and now I have no
mamma." - . :â -..
Mrs. Caswell has been sick for a long time ahd has recently been de
spondent, though her husband had no reason to fear that she would take her
life. When he left home his wife was in her usual mood and gave no hint
of the fearful tragedy she was contemplating. Mrs. Caswell was 33 years of
age and the daughter of Mrs. Van Hoosear of Castro Valley.
THE SAN FBAISrCISCO CALL, THURSDAY, APRIL 13, 1899.
YOUNG WIFE
CONFESSED TO
EVIL-DOING
Promised to Reform if
Given a Chance.
CHILDREN TAKEN AWAY
> - .
SIGNED AN AGREEMENT TO BE
HAVE THREE MONTHS.
The Tempter Came Along and at Once
the Pledge Was Broken Hu
sband Abducts the
Boy.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, April 12.
Mrs. Carrie Reuter could not behave
herself three months even to Insure for
her the custody of her two little children
of whom she is very fond. In court to
day Charles Reuter, who was divorced
from his wife some months ago, ap
peared to answer a charge of abducting
his little boy, 6 years old. Mrs. Reuter
charged that her ex-husband had gone
to the place where she was keeping the
child and taken it away in violation of
the decree of the court.
Reuters attorneys practically ended the
case by producing a little document,
which was an agreement made by Mrs.
Reuter on February 16 of this year, stat
ing that she would behave herself and
leave the red wine alone for three months
If put on probation and allowed to retain
her little one. Mrs. Reuter Is a comely
young woman, 25 years of age, and until
a tempter crossed her path and taught
her the habit of wine drinking she was
as good a young mother as any husband
could desire, according to the testimony.
The agreement produced in court does
not mince words. It opens with a con
fession by Mrs. Reuter that she had been
guilty of conduct which made her an un
fit person to' look after her children, but
she promised to quit bad acquaintances
and reform if given the chance to do so.
Her ex-husband gave her the chance. A
week ago he became possesed of Informa
tion which caused him to tremble for
his children's welfare, so he went to Mr*.
Reuters house, found his children dirty
and in rags, although he had "regularly
paid a stipulated amount for their main
tenance, and found the most undesirable
company ln the house. He took the chil
dren away. This morning he produced
them in court neatly dressed and look
ing very attractive. Evidently Mrs. Reu
ter had not told her attorney of the con
fession and agreement she made last Feb
ruary, for when It was sprung by Reuter
the mother's lawyer asked to see it and
at once requested the court to grant a
continuance. The case Is such that Ren
ter is confident he will be allowed to keep
the boy, although on account of Its ten
i der age the mother may be permitted for
1 a while to keep the baby girl.
HUNTING AND FISHING
ON MARIN COUNTY LANDS
The Supervisors of Marin County will
meet to-day in San Rafael, and among
matters that will claim the attention pf j
the board will be the proposed ordinance j
which, if passed, will prohibit the ship- |
ment of fish and game out of Marin i
County.
According to the State game laws the
Supervisors of the' different counties have
the power to ordain thai fish or game
shall not be shipped out of their county,
but when the Legislature vested that
power with the Supervisors it did not say
that game or fish when ln possession of
any person shall be subject to confisca
tion.
The intention of the Legislature at the
suggestion of the sportsmen of the State
was to stop the destruction of game and
lish by those who hunt and fish for the
markets, and with that object in view it
gave the Supervisors of the different
counties of the State the power to pass
ordinances to prevent the shipment of
game or fish out of their respective coun
ties.
Some of the leading counselors of this
city who were addressed on the subject
said that the Supervisors have the power
to curtail the shipment of game, but that
the confiscation of game or fish in season
by a constable or county officer when it
Is in possession of a sportsman or an an
gler returning from an outing -would be
an act conflicting with the general game
law of the State. The Supervisors of
Marin County can close the shooting sea
son to all sportsmen if they feel so dis
posed, but they cannot make a distinc
tion by placing a prohibitory measure on
sportsmen or anglers who do' not reside
in the county. Such an act would, ac
cording to legal opinion, be decidedly un
constitutional. â  *..
Doubtless the enemies of the Country
and Tamalpais Sportsmen's clubs are the
instigators of this latest move in matters
that pertain to fish and game, but the
Supervisors of Marin County, if they had
the power to shut off outsiders from en
joying the pleasures of the chase, would,
in the opininon of many residents of the
county, be inflicting a great injustice upon
the hotel proprietors and stable-owners of
the county, many of whom reap rich har
vests from visiting sportsmen and anglers
during the open seasons for fishing and
hunting.
Return of Rosenthal.
The return of the great pianist, Morltz
Rosenthal, to this city for two farewell
matinee recitals at the Grand Opera
house on the afternoons of Tuesday and
Wednesday, April 25 and 26, Is already at
tracting a full quota of attention at the
hands of local music lovers, and from the
present outlook the two recitals will be
attended by audiences calculated to crowd
the immense theater. Those who were
unable to secure seats for the last recitals
given here by Rosenthal will no doubt
be early in line to secure reservations,
and It will no doubt serve them well, as
all those who have already heard the
wizard of the piano will once more at
tend, and as the prices have been cut In
half for the coming engagement all
classes of music lovers will find it pos
sible to enjoy the concerts. Rosenthal's
return here will precede his sailing for the
Antipodes on his tour of the world. In
Australia his tour will include all the
larger cities, and already the managers
ln the land of the kangaroo are said to
vie with one another to secure the pianist.
MURPHY STOPPED
A RUNAWAY HORSE
A runaway occurred on Market street
yesterday afternoon about 2 o'clock, and
for a few minutes It looked as if there
would be urgent need for the ambulance
or Morgue wagon. Had it not been for
the prompt and brave action of Policeman
Murphy serious accident might have re
sulted. The animal, which was a big bay
nag full of ginger and spirit, started to
run from Lotta's fountain and dashed up
the street as far as Grant avenue, de
molishing one buggy that was left stand
ing close to the curb on the south side of
the street, and nearly' killing another
horse that was attached to a light buggy
which was also standing near the curb a
few yards further up the street.
The runaway belonged to M. W. Bode,
who lives at 2201 Fulton street, and was
attached to a light business buggy. When
Bode left the buggy he fastened the horse
with a light rope to a hitching pest In
front of the building." A band of music
passed down Market street, and the ani
mal becoming frightened started on a
mad run up the street. The thoroughfare
was crowded with pedestrians, who flew
in all directions to escape being knocked
down and trampled upon.
Opposite Grant avenue the maddened
animal shied off to the south side of the
street, nearly banging into a passing car.
The rear end of the buggy swerved sud
denly, knocking down a horse that was
standing near the curb and nearly break
ing his leg. The collision stopped the
frightened animal for a moment, but be
fore anybody could reach the spot the
horse started off again and ran a few
yards further on, where he collided with
another animal. Both horses fell -down,
but the force of the collision sent them
both sliding along the cement sidewalk,
scattering women and children in all di
rection. Before Bode's horse could re
gain his footing Policeman Murphy threw
himself upon his neck and pinned him
down to the sidewalk.
RIODE JANEIRO IN PORT.
The Pacific Mail steamer Rio de Janeiro
arrived late last night and anchored in
the stream. She left Hongkong on March
14 and Honolulu on April 5. and carried
with her thirty cabin passengers sixteen
European steerage and 171 Chinese and
Japanese. Everything is reported quiet
at Honolulu, but there Is an unusual ac
tivity ln the stock market. Since ihe an
nexation stocks of all kinds have been
steadily going up and there appears to be
no limit to the prices which will be paid.
The cargo on the Rio is also unusually
heavy. She carries 2800 tons of freight,
among which are 3800 bunches of bananas,
195 bundles of silk, 1700 packages of rice
and 81 bags of mail.
Among the passengers Is W. .F. Rice,
deputed to visit Japan as a special agent
of the Bureau of Immigration and Labor.
In the Divorce Court.
Stella Aldrich was granted a divorce
from Claude B. Aldrich by Judge Heb
bard yesterday on the ground of extreme
cruelty. Emma Esterberg has been
granted a divorce from Gustav Esterberg
on the ground of willful neglect. Suits
for divorce have been filed by Cora L.
Mitchell against William K. Mitchell for
failure to provide, and Joseph Lebade
against Katherine Labade for desertion.

the San Francisco Bar. An Introduction
on the nature of municipal charters pre
cedes the charter itself, which has been
carefully annotated with a number of
cases cited besides code and constitu

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for furnishing all the furniture^
fixtures, finishes, etc., lor the Hall of Justice
- a:.-. Morgue buildings.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of Super-
visors of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, April 4. 1899.
In accordance with resolution No. 2587
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervisors.
sealed proposals will be received in open ses-
sion of the Board of Supervisors on MON-
DAY AFTERNOON. April 17, 1899, from 2:30
to 3 o'clock, for furnishing all the .furniture,
fixtures, finishes, etc.. for the Hall of Justice
and Morgue buildings, in strict accordance
with the plans and specifications prepared by
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, and filed in
the office of the clerk of the Board of Super-
visors on April 3. 1899, copies of which can
be seen by Intending bidders at office of
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, at No. 26
Montgomery street.
Bidders will bid on this work with the un-
derstanding that all the material mentioned
ln the specifications shall be manufactured in
the State of California, and that all labor
necessary In the manufacture of said mate-
rial and all labor necessary in the erection
and completion of the work mentioned In the
specifications shall be performed by parties
residing In the State of California.
And It is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
persons employed on the above work, and
that the minimum rate of wages to be paid
shall be two ($2) dollars per day.
Bidders will also be required to submit with
their bids a certified check on some bank in
the city and county of San Francisco in a
sum equal to one-tenth (1-10) of the amount
of their bid, and the party or parties to
whom the contract or contracts may be
awarded will be required to enter into a bond
in a sum equal to one-fourth (hi) 0 _ the
amount of their contract, signed by two or
more good and sufficient sureties, to be ap-
proved by the Board of Supervisors, for the
faithful performance of tne contract
Bidders will estimate and state a price on
the following subdivisions of the material to
be supplied and work performed, to wit'
Firstâ For all furniture, including all metal
grill work In connection with same; metal
railings for prisoners' docks; all courtroom
and other railings; hardware; metal shelvin"
and vault doors; ventilators. Bertillon sys"
tern: photographic appliances and kitchen fur-
niture. ~- ,
Secondâ For gas and electric combination
fixtures. â 'â â â â¢'_' .'",;â â '*:
Thirdâ For water filtering system.
Fourthâ For tinting and decorating.
Fifthâ electrical apparatus, switch
board and telephone system.
Bidders will , bid with the distinct under-
standing that separate contracts will be
awarded for each class to the lowest respon-
sible bidder. _.â â¢; _-â  - ' **""
For any further Information required as to
receiving copies of plans and specifications
bidders will apply to the architects, Messrs
Shea & Shea.
The party or parties to whom each one of
the contracts may be awarded will be re
quired, prior to or at the time of the execu-
tion of said contracts, to pay one-fifth the
cost of advertising this and a former notice
ln three daily newspapers, and each success-
ful bidder to also pay the cost of the publi-
cation in the offlclal newspaper of the reso-
lution awarding him his contract.
In order to preserve uniformity and to fa-
cilitate the award the Board has resolved to
receive no bid unless made upon blank forms
prepared by the committee, and a certified
check on some batik In the city and county
of San Francisco in a sum equal to one-
tenth (1-10) of the bid deposited by the bid-
der with his bid and made payable to the
clerk of the Board of Supervisors, conditioned
if the proposal be accepted and the contract
awarded, and if the bidder shall fall or
neglect to pay the printing charges, execute
the contract and give the required bond with-
in six days after the award is made, then and
In that case the said sum shall be collected
and paid into the City and County Treasury
by said clerk as liquidated damages for such
" failure and neglect.
Blanks furnished by the clerk.
The Board reserves' the right to reject all
bids if the public good so requires.
JNO A. RUSSELL, Clerk.

LEES DEFENDS THE
COURSE OF PRICE
Says He Is Innocent of the
Charge of Corruption.
The Police Commissioners Commence
Their Investigation of the
Chinatown Scandal.
THE portentous "sack" which is Bald
to exist in Chinatown and which,
it Is Claimed, has tempted Lieuten
ant Price and his squad was the
subject of Investigation by the Po-
lice Commislsoners last evening.
For several hours the Commissioners lis
tened to the reading of the report of Chief
Lees, "which is calculated to exculpate
Price and his men of the charges of cor
ruption which have been brought against
them.
At 11 o'clock the head of the Police De
partment had not concluded reading his
report, and as Commissioner Tobin was
not feeling well he suggested that ihe in
vestigation be continued until next Mon
day night. A motjon to that effect was
made by Commissioner Alvord and im
mediately carried.
The Chief's report, as far as he read,
was a resume of the work done by Price
ln the Chinese quarter. Several months
ago, ho claimed, a number of Chinese
merchants waited on him and asked that
Price be sent to subdue the disorderly ele
ment In Chinatown. As a number of
wanton murders of inoffensive Chinamen
had been committed. Lees sent Price to
assist Sergeant McManus, who was then
in charge of the Chinese quarter. Shortly
afterward McManus was relieved at his
own request and Price instructed to take
full charge.
The Chief declared that Price's work
ln Chinatown was eminently satisfactory
to him. He gave a list of gambling joints
Price had broken up, and reiterated that
as far as his investigation went ho had ab
solutely no proof that either Price or his
men were guilty of corrupt i practices.
The dally reports of Price and his men. he
said, showed that they had made more
arrests and collected more money than
any other sergeant and squad who have
been In charge of Chinatown.
"In 1894," continued the Chief, .reading
from his report, "Lieutenant Esola. who
had been in Chinatown for two months,
was withdrawn, as the Commissioners
and myself had an abundance of evidence
showing that he could not make a raid
without being accompanied by Andrew
Lawrence, managing editor Of the Ex
aminer. Lawrence was with him nearly
all the time, and as there were some ugly
rumors floating around which did not re
flect much credit on the editor in ques
tion, I decided, after a consultation with
the Commissioners, to withdraw Esola.
Lawrence in his paper severely criticized
the Commissioners and the department in
general after that, but as I knew I had
beaten him at his own game I paid no at
tention to his diatribes.
"Shortly after Price's character was as
sailed he visited my office and demanded
an Investigation. He vigorously denied
that he was guilty of the charge of cor
ruption and pleaded with me to withdraw
him from Chinatown. I assured him of
my belief in his honesty and told him to
continue as he had been doing until such
time as the Commissioners Investigated
the charges preferred against him. Dur
ing my secret investigation I learned that
an ex-convict named Thomas Droulet was
responsible for some of the stories re
flecting on Price and the men which were
published in the Examiner. Droulet, who
is also known as Kelly, is a Chinatown
bum. Ho has served several terms in
San Quentin and Is a confirmed thief.
Some time ago he was thrown out of a
window by a woman who Is known as the
"Galloping Cow" and sustained a. fracture
of the skull. Since then he has been a
driveling Idiot.
-'Gambling has materially decreased
since Price has been in Chinatown." con
tinued the head of the department,
"reading from his voluminous report."
"I understand that it has been in
creased." interjected Commissioner To
bin. ln a rather sarcastic tone.
"we will prove that in court or any
where else," hotly replied the Chief.
"It was called to my attention," con
tinued Lees, "that Price was issuing
COLE FOUND HE
WHS NOT THE
WHOLE BOARD
School Directors Sup
press Him.
Chairman J. B. Cole of the committee
on janitors of the Board of Education, had
impressed upon him at the regular meet
ing last night that a committeeman Is not
the whole committee nor is a committee
the board. In the course of the evening's
business, when the reports were being
called for in their regular order. Secretary
Simpson called for that on janitors.
Mr. Cole, as chairman of the committee,
presented a report recommending several
dismissals and appointments of others in
the place of those deposed. Mr. Cole, the
proposer, cast aside recognized customs
and moved its adoption. On its face the
report seemed all right, but Director Hol
brook asked by whom It was signed. This
elicited the information that Mr. Cole
alone stood sponsor for it. Directors Head
and Conlan declining to be parties to its
recommendations. President Bergerot j
asked for the majority report of the com- j
mittee, but none was forthcoming. There- !
upon he declined to entertain the report
presented.
Director Cole tried to explain in an in
coherent manner that he had presented
the report to his colleagues for their sig
natures, but that they had declined to Join '
him in his recommendations. " He ex
plained that some Janitors were on the list
of dismissals upon the recommendations
of principals of schools. That from long
years of service they acted as though
they could do as they pleased in neglect
ing their duties. Others were included in
the list because he had evidence of their
neglect He spent several days in the
month on a tour of investigation, and
found that the rooms In a number of
buildings had not received proper care*.
President Bergerot dealt firmly but
kindly with the Director. He would not
permit the report to be presented. The
remaining ten members were a unit in
their views of the matter, hence there
will be no changes among the Janitors for
the present. â¢
The session of the board was a short
one. The important committees had
nothing to report, so the proceedings, aft
er the Cole incident, were merest routine.
The committee on rules recommended
that leave of absence be granted to: the
following teachers: Mrs. B. Linehan, Miss
K. H. Paxton, Miss B. Levingston, Miss
N. E. Ewlng, Miss M. A. Manning and
Mrs. M. A. Hogan.
Applications were received from the fol
lowing teachers asking that they be ex
cused from attending the Teachers' Insti
tute now in session: Walter S. Braun, A_
H. McDonald, Ellen Castiehun, Jennie L.
Powers, A. E. Roberts. A. J. Dannan
baum, C. S. Hendry, Smith O'Brien. John
McHenry, William Drew. James P. Bal
loun, Charles L. Turner, Miss Jennie Kie
nan.
Demands for salary were received from
Rose M. Stack and Mrs. Morrisey and
were referred to the finance committee.
A petition for a new school was received
cards to Chines, restaurant keepers, al- '
lowing them to continue playing music j
until 1 o'clock in the morning. The cards
bore the "name of the restaurants and
were signed by the lieutenant. I sent for
him and demanded to know why he had
overriden the law to the extent of allow
ing music to be played after midnight:
He explained that the Chinese were hold
ing their annual festivities, It being their
New Year, and that he saw no harm in
it. I told him it was against the law
and he promised that it would not occur
again.
"To show what schemes the Chinese will
resort to, I deem It proper to make pub
lic a forgery which was evidently In
tended to entrap me. On March 15, 1898,
I received a note purporting to have been
signed by the Consul General asking
the privilege of exploding lir.crackers be
tween certain hours on the following day.
I suspected that the signature was a for
gery, and, calling in my clerk, dictated a
reply to him, which was to be sent to the
Consul General, In a few days I received
an answer in which the Consul General
declared that the original letter was a
forgery and excusing tho crime by say
ing that one of his servants must have
written the request for the benefit of
some friend."
The Chief was about to descant on the
scheming methods of the Chinese in gen
eral when Commissioner Tobin, who nad
evidently grown tired while listening to
the Chief's report, suggested that further
hearing of the report be continued until
Monday night, which was adopted.
Before taking up the investigation of
Price and his men the Commissioners
tried Policeman John Crowley, charged
with failing to report for duty. On March
19 Crowley- visited a restaurant on Davis
street, where he met Sergeant Wolf and
two ladles. Crowley was unable to re
port for duty at midnight and at his re
?uest Sergeant Wolf went to the Call
ornia-street station, where he informed
the officer In charge that Crowley was
sick and unable to do patrol duty.
The matter was reported to Lieutenant
Blrdsall, who sent Sergeant Monahan to
investigate the story of Crowley's alleged
sickness.
Monahan visited his house and was sur
prised at not finding him there. Con
vinced that there was something wrong,
Captain Wittman preferred charges
against the officer. Officer Edwards tes
tified that he saw Wolf, Crowley, another
man and three women standing at the
corner of California .and Kearny streets a
short time before the watch reported on.
He reported the fact to acting Desk Ser
geant Young. The latter notified Lieu
tenant Blrdsall, who sent one of his men
to Crowley's house. The witness denied
that he said that the women were "hay
bags" or Barbary Coast rounders.
Officer Young testified that Crowley was
accompanied by a woman named Mrs.
Mclntyre. In answer to Commissioner
Gunst's questions, he said that the char
acter of the woman was bad. Captain
Wittman, when sworn, said that Crow
ley had told him that the Mclntyre wo
man was "stuck" on him, and that he
couldn't "shake" her.
Commissioner Gunst rather testily
asked why the case had not been brought
to the attention of the Police Commission
before.
"This offense occurred on the 20th of
last month," he remarked. "I should like
to know why the charges were not in
vestigated at the last meeting."
Chief Lees replied that he had read the
charges and expected that they would
be brought up shortly after the occur
rence.
"I spoke of the case at the recent meet
ing." remarked Gunst. "I would like to
know who is to blame for this seeming
negligence."
"Captain W right had the charges,"
hotly retorted Lees.
"Excuse me," interrupted Gunst. "It
must be Wright's mistake."
Alter listening to the statements of sev
eral witnesses to the effect that Crowley
was sick and not drunk, the Commission
ers decided. to dismiss the charge against
him.
from Improvement Club No. 1 of San

A Disastrous Bicycle Collision.
While scorching down the grade on
| Washington street, near Leavenworth,
' last night Ferdinand Lecam, a young
bicyclist rider, collided with J. C.
Andrews, an aged blacksmith, and as a
result both parties are lying at the Re
ceiving Hospital, suffering from slight
concussions of the brain, both having
been thrown on their heads. Lecam did
not recover consciousness until late in the
night, and then he was unable to give
i any account of the accident. As soon as
the young rider is able to be moved he
will be charged with battery.

Soldiers Entertained.
Last evening a reception was tendered
jto the members of the Twenty-fourth
â  Regiment, now at the Presidio, by the
, Canby Garrison No. 114, R. A. and N. U.,
!at California Hall. This is the first dem
onstration which has been tendered the
I boys since their arrival in this city.
The event of the evening was a speech
by Rev. Tillman Brown, who was fol
, lowed by Charles H. Tinsley, commander
lof the garrison, and other speakers It
was an Informal affair, and the time was
spent in entertainment which made the
soldiers feel welcome on the coast.

PRETTY GIRLS IN TABLEAUX
AT THE GINGERBREAD FETE
Artistic Booths Stock
ed With Dainty Bits,
Where the Visitor
and His Money
May Soon Be
Parted.
C ,- -)HK gingerbread fete for the
\ benefit of the Children's Hos
pital was Inaugurated yester
l day afternoon under the most
pleasant surroundings in Gold
en Gate Hall. The afternoon ; hardly
found the ladies prepared to entertain
their guests, but by evening every
thing was in perfect order and run
ning as smoothly as though tho enter
tainment was a full week. old.
For the opening day it was a splen
did attendance. Fashion has lately
decreed that I openings are no longer
the* very swellest affairs, and judging
by that it is evident that before the
fete comes to Its happy conclusion the
hall will be unable to hold those as
sembled, and that money will pile up
and fill to overflowing the capacious
coffers of the. worthy charity.
The programme arranged for the
evening went off with remarkable
success. The "Clippings From Life"
and "Miniatures" ; were beautifully
posed, and the curtain was parted
again and again to the merry tune of
palm to palm thunder.
The arrangements of the hall com
bine at once beauty and comfort, the
booths ranged round the walls, making
a background of brilliant colors. From
the door where you hand in your paste
board to the stage that bounds the
further extremity of the hall Is one
long line of artistic effects.
Variety must- have been the watch
word of the skillful decorators that
converted the plain bare hall into the
gorgeous fete. No one booth resem
bles the other. Each has a distinctive
feature particularly. appropriate to its
kind, making comparison -impossible.
One booth is only prettier than an
other Judged from an individual stand
point, and there is enough diversity to
please all tastes. More than a dozen
booths filled with' comely matrons and
fascinating belles appealed to the fash
ionable assemblage- and each booth
did a thriving business. -
In addition to the booths there are
the art gallery, with its wealth of rare
art works; the "hospitable table," a
miniature reproduction of the bene
fited institution, and looked after by
Mrs. Merrill, the Misses Leontine and
Juliette Smith, the. Misses Hughes and
Miss Bates; the wonderful doll house,
for which little Whittier Bothin dis
poses of raffle tickets, and the Bedouin
tent, with its Oriental Inscription, be
neath * which the palmist reads your
past and predicts wonders for the
future. Miss McNeil read the . palms
last- evening, but to-night the great
Foeselli will be on hand. .
The programme in full yesterday
evening was as follows:
